==PAGE:3==
 
iii 
Abstract 
 
The application of environmental assessment tools, such as LCA, has become of strategic importance 
within the manufacturing industry to study and quantify the environmental performances of the entire 
life cycle of products. Already from the very beginning of the product development process, with 
assumptions and estimations, it is possible to assess the environmental profile of a product and to 
reduce drastically its potential impacts on the environment. This study was conducted in collaboration 
with Primewool Holding Denmark, a company specialized in thermal insulation solutions using 
paper-wool recycled from plasterboard and paper-based C&D waste as insulating material. Currently, 
the R&D department of Primewool is working on the development of innovative thermal insulation 
batts made of paper-wool. The aim of this master thesis is to develop the first prototypes and to assess 
them from an environmental point of view also considering the production costs. The obtained results 
are intended to support and guide further the development process and to give an insight and a wider 
overview of the potential environmental impacts generated by the choices made during the product 
development phase. LCA is used as tool for the environmental assessment, while an adapted LCC is 
applied for the production costs analysis. Experiments were conducted to study and analyse possible 
binders to implement in the production of the insulation batts. From the tests four different insulation 
batts compositions (prototypes) and two different building methods (i.e. dry - layers approach and 
wet - pulp approach) were chosen to undergo the environmental and economic assessment. The LCA 
of the four compositions were modelled in SimaPro and the deep analysis of the results obtained gave 
insight for recommendations to Primewool Holding Denmark. The study of the two possible 
manufacturing methods showed that the R&D department should give priority to the dry process. 
Great weight should also be given to the choice of the binders due to their high influence on the 
overall impacts. The upcycling of a waste into an insulation product was reflected in the impact scores 
of the models; positive impacts (benefits) were observed in the four systems under study especially 
in the midpoint impact categories Climate change and Fossil depletion. Of the four compositions 
analysed, the best solution detected, from both an environmental and economic point of view, was 
built with paperwool, kenaf fibres and cellulose ether as binders and assembled with the layers 
approach (dry process). For this composition, the savings due to the recycling of the C&D waste and 
production of paper-wool are more substantial than the impacts due to the production of the insulation 
batt in all the three damage categories at endpoint level. This study is intended to conduct and support 
the first phase of the development process, underlining the significant value of considering the 
environmental performances from the very beginning of the product development. It is from the 
outcomes of this work that the product development should proceed further considering also new 
binders and implementing the delivered LCA models.

Preface 
This work represents the conclusion of my two years Master of Science in Environmental Engineering 
at Danmarks tekniske universitet (DTU). The “Primewool” project has been carried out in 
collaboration with Primewool Holding Denmark A/S and the Department of Management 
Engineering. The timeframe of this Master thesis extended from the 23rd of January to the 23rd of July 
2017 for a total of 5 months. This work counts for 30 ECTS. The project was supervised by Stig 
Irving Olsen, associate professor at DTU and Henrik Lund-Nielsen, representative from the external 
company.

Chapter 1 
 
Introduction 
 
This master thesis focuses on the development of innovative thermal insulation batts made of paper-
wool and on the subsequent assessment from an environmental and economic point of view. The 
introductory chapter describes and presents the main topics related and covered in this thesis. Starting 
from the presentation of the company, where this project was thought through, are then presented the 
concepts of circular economy, upcycling and solid waste with focus on the C&D waste category. 
Subsequently, the main stages constituting and structuring this master thesis project are briefly 
presented in order to give to the reader, from the very beginning, a wide picture of this work. 
 
1.1 The company and the project 
 
Primewool Holding Denmark A/S is a 
company 
operating 
in 
Denmark, 
specialized in thermal insulation solutions 
for private houses. The Primewool project 
has been funded by the ECO-innovation 
Initiative of the European Union together 
with private investors. It started some years 
ago with the aim of turning paper-based 
waste 
from 
the 
construction 
and 
demolition sector into a thermal insulation 
product. The research and development 
activities carried out by Primewool have realized the potential to turn the paper-based waste into a 
cellulose insulation product, i.e. paper-wool. With this new solution, the paper-based C&D waste 
does not need to be landfilled nor incinerated, but can be recycled, adding new value to a material 
that formerly was considered and treated as a waste. Even though the company started with the idea 
of recycling all the paper-based waste coming from the C&D sector, for economic and technical 
reasons, in the last years it specialized on the treatment of gypsum plasterboard waste. In this way, 
apart from the recycling of the paper fraction, also a high amount of gypsum is recovered while the 
waste is treated. Two different recycling units were developed to work in succession, a gypsum 
recycling unit and a paper recycling unit. The recycled gypsum powder obtained from the gypsum 
recycling unit simply re-enters the cycle of the gypsum production, where is mixed with other gypsum 
and used in an environmentally friendly production of virgin products. While the paper fraction 
obtained from the paper recycling unit is fiberized and recycled (upcycled) into an insulation material.  
 
Right now, the main product commercialized by Primewool is the loose wool insulation for direct 
blowing. This service is oriented towards private houses, especially old ones, where the thermal 
insulation precautions are low or null.  One of the main issues in the traditional cellulose 
Figure 1.1: Primewool Holding Denmark A/S Logo. 
==PAGE:18==
 
 
Chapter 1. Introduction 
 
2 
manufacturing is the compacting of the paper due to transportation reasons. In fact, the compaction 
process would decrease drastically the thermal capacity and insulation capability of the paper-wool. 
To overcome this unproductive characteristic, a new supply method has been developed by 
Primewool, assuring that the fiberized paper is not compressed all the way through the installation. 
The fiberized paper-wool is loaded into cages on wheels to ensure an efficient handling (Figure 1.2). 
These cages can be easily loaded into containers assuring efficient transport by using traditional and 
cost effective transportation means. From there, the cages can be delivered to the installation vehicle. 
Although, due to the lack of compaction of the produced cellulose wool, the supply system would 
lead to a marginal higher transport cost, this is of limited final consequence as the production of 
Primewool is characterized by local manufacture, close to where the waste is generated and where 
the insulation product is used. 
 
Figure 1.2: Transportation of the paper-wool in mobile cages to the location of the installation. 
As displayed in the following pictures, with the innovative blowing technology the paper-wool is 
installed directly both in cavity of walls (i.e. in between double walls) and lofts. The work is done by 
trained installers (see Figure 1.3).  
 
Figure 1.3: Installation of paper-wool as insulant on an attic (om the left) and in cavity between walls (on the right) 
==PAGE:19==
Chapter 1. Introduction 
 
3
Especially in old houses, no insulation precautions were taken, and the cavity in between walls was 
left completely empty, and only air was used as insulant. In these cases, the Primewool technology 
can be used to fill the cavity with paper-wool guaranteeing high insulation performances with the 
usage of recycled raw materials. The following pictures (Figure 1.4) show the installation of the 
product in a cavity wall. When the installation is complete, the holes where the paper-wool was blown 
in are simply sealed and no marks or damages are visible. 
 
 
Figure 1.4: Installation by blowing of paper-wool in a cavity between two external walls (before, during and after). 
Right now, other products are under development and not yet commercialized. One of those are the 
insulation batts made of paper-wool developed and studied during this master thesis project. Since 
the application of the loose wool insulation for direct blowing is limited to old houses with cavity 
walls and to attics, it was decided to develop thermal insulating batts made of paper-wool suitable for 
other thermal insulation applications, such as direct installation on inner walls. The main component 
of the insulation batts would be the paper-wool, recycled from the C&D paper-based waste, already 
tested and certified as an effective insulant. Binders would be needed to keep the paper-wool together 
and produce the wanted shape of the insulation batt. From the beginning of the development process 
together with the traditional influencing factors of the product development such as technical and 
economic feasibility also the environmental aspects were taken into account in order to create an 
insulation batt with a competitive eco-friendly profile and with low environmental impacts. It is 
important to note that it is outside the objectives of this master thesis to develop a final product ready 
for the commercialization. The project focused on the first part of the product development process, 
and the outcomes of this thesis will be used by the R&D department of Primewool Holding Denmark 
A/S to further continue the development of the product. 
 
 
1.2 Circular economy & Upcycling 
 
Circular economy is a term, with conceptual roots in industrial ecology, used to define a designed 
economic system able to regenerate and restore itself (Andersen, 2007). The final aim is to minimize 
resources usage and implement cleaner technologies, keeping always products and materials at their 
highest value and utility (Ellen MacArthur Foundation, 2017). In accordance with Ellen MacArthur 
Foundation (2013) in a circular economy all the material flows can be grouped in two cycles: the 
biological and technical one. In the former, biological materials are able to be reintegrated in the 
biosphere, in the latter the materials are recycled and restored without ending in the biosphere. Three 
are the principles on which the concept of circular economy is built on: (i) preserve and enrich natural 
==PAGE:20==
 
 
Chapter 1. Introduction 
 
4 
resources, (ii) optimize resources usage and (iii) improve systems efficiency (Ellen MacArthur 
Foundation, 2017). In accordance with Andersen (2007), the concept of circular economy is supposed 
to bring benefits to the society both from an environmental and economic point of view. Benefits will 
be obtained by minimizing the amounts of materials disposed to the environment, and by increasing 
the efficiency of the materials usage. It is important to note that, even if the potential benefits appear 
straightforward, the leading perspective in the circular economy concept is from a physical rather 
than from an economic point of view (Andersen, 2007). The circular economic system is not able to 
perform recycling in perpetuity, since at some stages and in specific circumstances recycling will be 
too difficult and unfavourable both from an economic and environmental point of view (Andersen, 
2007). A clear example of application of this concept is the recycling of the gypsum from the 
plasterboard waste during the production of paper-wool. In this way, the segregated fraction re-enters 
directly the cycle of the gypsum production, avoiding partially the extraction of raw materials and 
the disposal into the biosphere.   
 
Linked to the concept of circular economy is the idea of upcycling in which the waste materials and 
products are not only recycled but, in the process, are given a higher value from a quality and 
environmental point of view (Pol, 2010; Zhou et al. 2014). The term upcycling was coined in 1994 
by the mechanical engineer Reiner Pilz during an interview conducted by Thornton Kay (Salvo, 
1994). Pol (2010) defined upcycling as “a constructive transformation of waste combining cost 
benefits and waste reduction”. In Primewool project, the plasterboard waste is not simply recycled 
but transformed in a more valuable product, improving in the upcycling process its functionality. The 
C&D paper-based waste is transformed in a new and sustainable product, i.e. paper-wool. It is 
important to note that the upcycling can either be technical, artistic or functional (Pol, 2010). 
 
1.3 Solid waste 
 
The solid waste management is a sector able to significantly influence two of the main global issues 
of our era, i.e. resource depletion and climate change (Laurent et al. 2014). Christensen (2011) defined 
waste simply as “a left-over, a redundant product or a material of no or marginal value for the owner 
and which the owner wants to discard”. Nowadays, differing from the past, waste is seen more and 
more as a resource with a potential value and no more as a burden to get rid of (Fudala-Ksiazek et 
al., 2016; Christensen, 2011). Since the types of waste produced vary a lot from category to category 
(e.g. industrial waste, household waste, construction and demolition waste) different solutions are 
needed to tackle efficiently the issue. However, a common approach to the waste management does 
exist: the waste hierarchy. It was already implemented by the Western word and part of Asia since 
1980s, and the name used for defining it may vary, for instance in Japan is called the 3R approach 
(i.e. reuse, recycle, recover), but the main concept and message behind is the same: avoid landfilling 
(Christensen 2011). In Figure 1.5 below the waste hierarchy as dictated by the Waste Framework 
Directive (2008/98/EC) and summarized by Christensen (2011) is presented together with the concept 
of circular economy. 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:21==
Chapter 1. Introduction 
 
5
 
 
 
Figure 1.5:The waste hierarchy and the circular economy, modified from Tellus (2017)  
 
The purpose of the waste hierarchy is to prioritize disposal methods based on their efficiencies in 
order to protect the environment, safeguarding raw materials and energy consumption. The actions 
are defined based on sustainability concepts and ordered from the most favourable to the least 
favourable. This hierarchy captures the order of preference for dealing with an effective waste 
management system, showing the different available options to minimize the amount of waste and 
get the maximum practical benefits (Pitt & Smith 2003; Hansen et al., 2002). The prevention is the 
first method suggested and aims to directly avoid the production of waste, minimizing the issue from 
the very beginning. However, the fact that this is not a realistic and far-reaching solution in the short 
term creates the need to find other options and ways to tackle the issue. In case where prevention is 
not possible the reuse and recycling of the materials are recommended, respectively. Both do not 
solve the waste issue itself but, for instance, these approaches contribute to the limitation of usage of 
virgin materials, giving a new life to the products. The fourth step is the recovery in terms of materials 
utilization and energy recovery (e.g. incinerator) (Christensen, 2011). And the last one, applied when 
none of the previous solutions are available, is the final disposal, i.e. landfilling and mass burning 
without recovery. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:22==
 
 
Chapter 1. Introduction 
 
6 
1.4 Construction & Demolition waste 
 
When talking about waste, in accordance with Christensen (2011) there are diverse categories in 
which the waste can be classified. In this thesis, the main focus is given to the construction and 
demolition (C&D) waste since all the materials taken into account and studied during the project 
belong to this category. 
 
C&D waste accumulates during the building, repairing, renovating or deconstructing of buildings and 
civil infrastructures (Christensen, 2011) and it accounts for approximately 25-30% of the total waste 
generated in the entire Europe (European Commission, 2017). This waste category is composed by a 
wide range of different and recyclable materials such as concrete, bricks, gypsum, wood, glass and 
metals (Christensen, 2011). Furthermore, C&D waste is one of the heaviest and most voluminous 
waste flows generated around Europe (European Commission, 2017). In the past, this waste category 
was considered harmless for the environment and for this reason landfilling was the disposal solution 
chosen. However, in the last decades it has been recognized that C&D waste may contain 
contaminated materials and consequently a more efficient and sustainable disposal was implemented 
(Christensen, 2011). The European Union considers C&D waste as a high priority waste stream for 
its high potential, not yet exploited, of recycling and reuse (European Commission, 2017). In fact, 
article 11.2 of the Waste Framework Directive (2008/98/EC) states that all the European countries 
shall achieve the goal of a 70% recycling rate (by weight) of non-hazardous C&D waste by 2020. 
Recycling is usually achieved either using the C&D waste directly as filling material (e.g. levelling 
of low lying areas, road base material or fill in harbour) or, after a sorting process, with the reuse of 
the raw materials. 
 
Although significant differences exist between the recycling levels of the various Member States still 
a high amount of C&D waste is landfilled in Europe. Even when some materials are recycled (e.g. 
concrete, bricks and wood) the paper wrapping of building products (i.e. the paper-based fraction of 
the C&D waste) is generally send to landfills or incineration. This is in clear contrast with the 
presented waste hierarchy, with the consequence of creating avoidable additional costs both for the 
environment and for the operating companies. It is within this issue that the Primewool project started 
some years ago with the aim of recycling and giving new life to the paper-based fraction from the 
C&D waste. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:23==
Chapter 1. Introduction 
 
7
1.5 Stages of the project 
 
As explained above, the master thesis project focused on the development of innovative thermal 
insulation batts made of paper-wool and on their assessment from an economic and environmental 
point of view. The following flow-chart (Figure 1.6) summarizes the main five stages in which the 
master thesis project can be divided: conception of the idea and testing through experiments of the 
possible solutions, selection of four compositions to assess and finally, analysis of the results and 
delivery of suggestions and recommendations to the R&D department of Primewool Holding 
Denmark A/S. 
 
 
 
Figure 1.6: The five stages in which the master thesis project can be divided. 
 
The first phase of the project was the conception of the idea of developing insulation batts from the 
already commercialized insulating cellulose fibres, i.e. paper-wool. During this phase, also a planning 
of the future runs of experiments was made, in which, throughout a literature research, the possible 
binders to implement were selected and the combinations to test were chosen. In the next phase, 
different experiments were performed in order to study and analyse the behaviour of the paper-wool 
and the efficiency of the different binders selected and of the compositions tested. Two manufacturing 
approaches were used to prepare the prototypes of the insulation batts as it will be presented in the 
experiments section. After the analysis of the obtained results, during the third phase, four different 
compositions of the thermal insulation batts were proposed for the two assessments. In the fourth 
phase, the four insulation batt compositions were assessed from an economical and environmental 
point of view. Considering the results of both the assessments and of the experiments, 
recommendations and suggestions will be proposed to the R&D department of Primewool Holding 
Denmark A/S about which direction continue to follow in the product development process and to 
support it.

Chapter 1. Introduction

==PAGE:25==
Chapter 2 
 
Methods 
 
In the following section, the two assessment frameworks applied during this master thesis are 
concisely presented with the aim to give a general understanding of the tools. Bearing in mind that 
the focus of the thesis are not the methods themselves but the application of those to the product 
development, they are only briefly presented and not analysed in great depth. 
 
2.1 Life Cycle Assessment 
 
Life cycle assessment is a framework used and implemented by engineers to assess and quantify 
environmental impacts of products and systems (Laurent, 2016). The framework and the detailed 
requirements were standardized by ISO 14040 and by ISO 14044, respectively (Klöpffer 2003; 
Laurent, 2016). For practitioners, there are also guidelines available such as the ILCD handbook by 
the European Commission (2010). Even though LCA is still an active field of research, in the last 
decade it has reached a mature growth in comparison with the other two components of Life Cycle 
Sustainability Assessment (LCSA), i.e. LCC (Life cycle costing) and SLCA (Societal life cycle 
assessment) (SETAC, 2011).  
 
In accordance with SETAC (2011) three are the principles differentiating LCA from the other 
available environmental assessment tools. First of all, during an LCA study the assessment is carried 
out on the entire life cycle of a product or a system (namely from cradle to grave), i.e. from the raw 
materials to the final disposal. Secondly, the usage of a functional unit is a peculiar characteristic of 
LCA, in fact, all the inputs of the system (e.g. raw materials, energy needed) and the outputs (e.g. 
emissions, byproducts) are scaled to a functional unit, i.e. a quantitative measure of the output 
analyzed. Finally, LCA results can be used as a comparative method between different products or 
systems performing similar functions and having a common functional unit. It is important to note 
that LCA studies are carried out also on singular product in order to assess their general environmental 
profile, possible future developments and find environmental hotspots of the entire system.  
 
As shown in Figure 2.1 below, the LCA framework is composed by five different steps (i.e. Goal & 
Scope definition, Inventory analysis, Impact assessment and Interpretation) and it can be directly 
applied for different purposes (e.g. Product development, Marketing, Public policy making). 
==PAGE:26==
 
Chapter 2. Methods 
 
10 
 
 
Figure 2.1: Life cycle assessment framework modified from EC, ILCD Handbook (2010). 
 
It is worth noting that in Figure 2.1 the arrows linking the different phases are pointing in both 
directions, e.g. from goal definition to scope definition and from the latter to the former. This is an 
important and peculiar feature, useful to underline the iterative nature of LCA. In fact, the LCA 
framework should not be seen as a linear process but rather as a continuous feedback loop between 
the different phases, composed by different iterations (Laurent, 2016).  
 
Goal & Scope definition are the first two phases of the LCA framework. Decisions made during those 
two phases influence the entire study since they are the foundations of the whole analysis, from the 
collection of data to the interpretation of the results. For what concerns Goal definition, six are the 
aspects to cover and address (European Commission, 2010):  
 
 Intended application of the results: where the planned use of the study is clearly stated (e.g. 
environmental hotspot analysis of a specific system, accounting study, policy information and policy 
development). 
 Limitations and restrictions due to methodological choices, important assumptions and 
environmental impact coverage: it is important to clearly state, for instance, which environmental 
impact assessment method is intended to implement and also which impact categories will be 
analyzed in order to contextualize the results. Also, the assumptions made during the study will 
influence the interpretation of the results (e.g. use phase disregarded in a study). 
 
==PAGE:27==
Chapter 2. Methods 
11
 Reasons for performing the study and decision-context: there is the need to state the motive and 
the cause of the study and also to specify in which decision context the study belongs. In accordance 
with the ILCD guidelines any LCA study should be classified into one of the three decision-context 
situations, i.e. A, B, C. A LCA study falls in Situation A and Situation B when there are decisions to 
be taken from the results, the difference in those two situations is the scale of the studied product 
system. In fact, the former is defined as “Micro-level decision support” the latter as “Macro-level 
decision support”. Studies falling in the decision-context C, defined as “Accounting”, are those not 
used to support decisions but performed for purely descriptive objectives. It is possible to differentiate 
this decision-context in two sub-categories, i.e. C1 and C2. The difference between decision-context 
C1 and C2 is the presence or not of interactions with other systems included in the model (European 
Commission, 2010).  
 Target audience of the deliverables/results: the identification of the audience to whom the results 
are addressed is essential for two main reasons, to identify the technical level of the report and to 
acknowledge the necessity of critical review. 
 Comparative studies to be disclosed to the public: it has to be clearly stated whether the study 
contains a comparative part including precise references to eventual competitor’s performances and 
detailed descriptions of the latter. Moreover, whether it is intended to be published or not influence 
the study itself and therefore has to be clearly specified. 
 Commissioner of the study and other influential actors: in the goal definition, the commissioner 
of the LCA has to be introduced together with all possible other contributing and participating 
stakeholders (e.g. experts involved in the study, NGOs, external consultants and academics, etc.). 
 
During the second phase of an LCA, named Scope definition, it is presented what is going to be 
analyzed during the assessment and the methodologies applied during the process. In this section nine 
are the aspects that have to be covered (European Commission, 2010; Laurent, 2016): 
  
 Types of deliverables: it is important to state in which format the results will be provided and 
presented to the commissioner (e.g. Comparative LCA study, LCIA results). 
 Object of the assessment: where the function, the functional unit and the reference flows are defined. 
It is important to note that this section is fundamental for all the further calculations carried out within 
the whole assessment. 
 LCI modelling framework and handling of multifunctional processes: in accordance with the 
Goal definition and the decision-context, defined previously, are hereby selected the modeling 
principles (i.e. attributional, consequential) and method approaches (i.e. allocation, system 
expansion) to be applied during the study. 
 System boundaries, completeness requirements, and related cut-off rules: the description of the 
system boundaries (i.e. the limits of the analyzed system) is necessary to assure that all the flows 
related to the system under study are given consideration. Moreover, defining clearly the cut-off 
criteria which will be applied is essential, for the audience, in order to understand the influence of 
those on the quality of the final outcomes of the assessment. 
 LCI data requirements and quality (technological, geographical, and time-related 
representativeness): in this section the quality of the gathered and used data is analyzed from a 
technological, geographical and temporal point of view; the higher the quality of the data applied and 
available, the more reliable will be the results obtained through the study. 
==PAGE:28==
 
Chapter 2. Methods 
 
12 
 The impact assessment criteria: both the impact assessment method and the impact categories, used 
subsequently for the environmental impact assessment phase, are defined and listed within this 
section. If implemented, the normalization and weighting methods have to be defined. 
 Special requirements for system comparisons: in case of direct comparison of two or more systems 
or products some additional aspects need to be considered in this part (e.g. usage of same functional 
unit to make the comparison fair). 
 Critical review needs: critical review is always performed by practitioners and experts external to 
the study and it is only mandatory in cases of publication of the results. 
 Planning reporting of results: hereby the form of the results delivered is discussed (e.g. detailed 
project report, condensed executive summary) together with inclusion and exclusion of sections of 
the study. 
 
The third phase of the LCA framework is the Inventory analysis, where all the inputs (e.g. raw 
materials, energy needed) and outputs (e.g. emissions, byproducts) of the studied system are gathered, 
quantified and scaled in accordance with the scope of the study and the previously defined functional 
unit (European Commission, 2010; Laurent, 2016). The phase following the Inventory analysis is the 
Impact assessment, where all the inputs and outputs analyzed previously are translate into 
environmental impacts. In accordance with ILCD handbook five are the steps of this phase, i.e. 
Selection of impact categories, Classification, Characterization, Normalization and Weighting, 
however only the first three are mandatory (Laurent, 2016). The final and last phase of the LCA 
framework is Interpretation, where the questions posed during the Goal definition are answered and 
the outcomes of the study is presented. In this phase, all the results obtained during the study are 
considered (e.g. environmental impacts, life cycle inventory), respecting the limitations imposed 
during the Goal & Scope definition, in order to achieve the aim of the study. 
 
 
2.2 Life Cycle Costing 
 
Contrarily to the LCA, Life Cycle Costing (LCC) has not been internationally standardized yet. Three 
types of LCC are available: Conventional LCC, Environmental LCC and Societal LCC (SETAC, 
2011). In general, its aim is to summarize all costs associated with a product or a system related to its 
entire life cycle, i.e. from cradle to grave. The LCC is a versatile tool and can be applied for different 
purposes, e.g. planning method, hotspots analysis, investment planning tool. As said, LCC can be 
conducted as single assessment, but also in support of a LCA or as a part of Life Cycle Sustainability 
Assessment (LCSA), i.e. with LCA and SLCA (Laurent, 2016; SETAC, 2011). When carried out 
together, LCC and LCA, similar system boundaries and functional unit are needed in order to have a 
meaningful outcome as result (Klöpffer 2008; Laurent, 2016; SETAC, 2011). In accordance with 
Klöpffer (2003) LCC should be seen as a valuable implementation of LCA, since sustainable and 
green products and services should be cost-effective and by combining those, designers and engineers 
are able to find the best suitable trade-off. When in a study both LCA and LCC are carried out together 
in parallel, during the LCC only real money flows are considered, in fact, no environmental costs 
(e.g. externalities) are included to prevent the overlapping and double counting between LCA and 
LCC (Klöpffer, 2003). As LCA, also LCC can have a comparative nature, also in this circumstance 
the usage of a common functional unit between the compared systems is needed (SETAC, 2011). It 
==PAGE:29==
Chapter 2. Methods 
13
is worth noting that both LCC and LCA study analyze systems in a steady-state (Klöpffer, 2008; 
Laurent, 2016). In accordance with SETAC (2011) from a time-related point of view, two different 
LCC can be defined: ex ante LCC and ex post LCC. The former is carried out during the first phases 
of a decision-making process and it is based on estimates and assumptions, the latter is applied on 
already completed projects or products where actual results and data are available. The target group 
of the study (e.g. producer, user, recycler or the entire life cycle chain) is of fundamental importance 
for the interpretation of the results which is the reason it has to be defined clearly during the Goal and 
Scope definition phase.  
 
LCC has the same framework structure as LCA, however the impact assessment part is substituted 
by the monetarization and aggregations of the total costs, expressed in one of the most common 
currencies per functional unit (Klöpffer, 2003). In accordance with SETAC (2011), three are the 
phases of an LCC carried out to support an LCA, i.e. Goal & Scope definition, Data collection and 
Interpretation. For what concerns Goal & Scope definition Laurent (2016) suggested to use the same 
approach dictated by ISO 14040/44 for LCA as a base and adjust it to fit the needs of the diverse 
cases. During the Data collection and the Inventory analysis it is important to consider not only all 
the material and energy flows, as in LCA, but the view needs to be stretched to cover all costs (e.g. 
indirect costs and immaterial costs). Finally, during the last phase, as for LCA, the results are 
presented, interpreted and analyzed. It is worth noting that performing a sensitivity analysis study 
during the Interpretation phase of an LCC is very important for the interpretation of the results. In 
fact, contrarily to LCA, commodity prices are much more volatile (e.g. fuel costs) (Laurent, 2016).

Chapter 2. Methods 
 
14 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:31==
 
Chapter 3 
 
Literature review 
 
In the following chapter, a literature review of the topics relevant to this master thesis is presented. 
The analysis starts with a presentation of the importance in the product development phase, of the 
environmental performances, underlying the potential benefits of the application of assessment tools 
as decision making support from the early phase of the process. It continues with a presentation and 
analysis of peer-reviewed case studies of current applications of LCA and LCC during the product 
development in a wide range of diverse industries, highlighting their peculiar versatility. Finally, the 
case studies are narrowed down to insulation products and materials, which provide the most valuable 
insights for this master thesis. 
 
3.1 The environmental factor in the product development 
 
In accordance with Nielsen & Wenzel (2002) the following phases (Figure 3.1) are the traditional 
steps of the product development process: idea, analysis, goal definition, concept development and 
detail development. 
 
 
Figure 3.1: Traditional steps of the product development process, modified from Nielsen & Wenzel (2002). 
 
Traditionally, the factors considered during the design of a new product were mainly economic, 
technical and aesthetic. In the past, the environmental performances, if taken into account during the 
product development, were always treated as a requirement to be fulfilled, a side factor or simply as 
additional costs. There was a general idea that environmental improvements were always linked with 
higher costs in the production (Nielsen & Wenzel, 2002; Bhander et al., 2003). However, this is not 
always the case. In fact, eco-friendly solutions can eventually reduce some of the costs, e.g. resources 
usage, or even bring direct benefits for the producers such as tax savings. The willingness of 
consumers towards more sustainable products has also a substantial impact from a marketing point 
IDEA
GOAL 
DEFINITION
DETAILS 
DEVELOPEMENT

CONCEPT 
DEVELOPEMENT

OF PRODUCTION
==PAGE:32==
Chapter 3. Literature review 
 
16 
of view (Nielsen & Wenzel, 2002). All those reasons not only underline but also highlight how the 
environmental performances during the development of a product should be seen as a contribution to 
its overall competitiveness and consequently as one of the main factors. Figure 3.2 below summarizes 
the factors influencing simultaneously the design of a new product during the development process 
(Bhander et al., 2003). 
 
 
Figure 3.2: Traditional factors influencing the product development process, modified from Bhander et al., (2003 
 
From Figure 3.2 above, it is clear that several are the factors affecting simultaneously the development 
of a new product. Sometimes, all those requirements are directly conflicting, challenging engineers 
and designers to find coexisting solutions (Schmidt, 2003). For instance, it is common knowledge 
that the usage of high quality materials to increase the durability of a product, directly increase the 
production costs and therefore the final competitiveness of the product. Therefore, engineers and 
designers are facing continuously a complex challenge in order to find the proportionate trade-off 
between those factors to optimize the product. 
 
During the last decades, the concept of eco-design has taken more and more the lead, and nowadays, 
during the product development process, the environmental properties are tied integrated together 
with the more traditional factors (e.g. economic, technical and aesthetic) in order to design 
competitive and sustainable products. The straightforward idea behind the eco-design concept is to 
decrease the environmental impacts of a product without compromising its function, quality and price 
(Bhander et al., 2003). The ISO-standard 14062 defined eco-design as “the integration of 
environmental aspects into product design and development” (ISO, 2002; Som et al., 2010). The 
European Union implemented some regulations in order to decrease the energy consumptions and the 
environmental impacts of the products sold around the European market. For instance, with the 
DURABILITY
SAFETY

TECHNOLOGY
ENVIRONMENTAL 
PERFORMANCE
STANDARDS & 
LEGISLATIONS
COSTS
MATERIALS&
DESIGN
==PAGE:33==
Chapter 3. Literature review 
17
European Union Ecodesign Directive (2009/125/EC), mandatory eco-design requirements were 
established for energy-related and energy-consuming products. The regulation focuses also on the 
materials used, in order to enhance the recyclability of the products (Auer et al. 2017). Ramani et al. 
(2010) highlighted how materials used and manufacturing choices are not the only influencing factors 
in the entire life cycle of the products, in fact also transportation, packaging, supply chain and ways 
of disposal have great influence on the overall environmental impacts of products. Even though 
guidelines push the producers towards the manufacturing of more sustainable products, the issues to 
tackle are often very wide and complex. To name few, bring together environmental considerations, 
product competition, functionality and aesthetic is an overwhelming task for designers and engineers. 
For this reason, efficient quantitative tools are needed to help and support developers making 
informed decisions during their work (Ramani et al., 2010). For what concerns the economic aspects 
during product development, Schmidt (2003) suggested that LCC has the potential power to influence 
and support decision makers during their work, quantifying the total costs over the life-time of a 
product (Auer et al., 2016; Kadarova et al., 2015; Klöpffer 2008). For the environmental aspects, the 
ISO-standardized tool LCA can be implemented by designers and engineers to quantify and evaluate 
the environmental impacts of their choices (Klopffer, 2003; Intrachooto et al., 2017; Nielsen & 
Wenzel, 2002; Auer et al., 2017; Hallstedt 2015; Bhander et al., 2003). In fact, the iterative 
characteristic of product development, LCC and LCA can be combined and seen as an integrated 
procedure (Nielsen & Wenzel, 2002; Schöggl et al., 2017; Bhander et al., 2003). 
 
Several authors highlighted the importance of applying LCA and/or LCC from the early stage of the 
product development process (Bhander et al., 2003; Schmidt, 2003; Intrachooto et al., 2017; Hallstedt, 
2017; Waage, 2007; Kaebernick et al., 2003). Moreover, Mascle & Zhao (2008) and Seo et al. (2002) 
claimed that the first part of the process is influencing up to 80% of the total costs of both production 
and usage during its lifespan, and consequently also the environmental impacts. For this reason, from 
the very beginning of the development process, the environmental performances have to be taken into 
account, together with the other influencing factors, since the design stage is determining the most 
substantial share of the impacts of the entire product life (Lagerstedt, et al., 2003). In fact, it is also 
easier to make changes in the design of a product while it is still under development rather than little 
adaptations when it is already on the market and in a complete production phase. In accordance with 
Bhander et al. (2003) the Design Degree of freedom, namely the number of possible solutions to adapt 
during the process, decrease drastically together with the potential of environmental improvements 
after each phase of the product development process. Figure 2.4 below presents this concept 
graphically. This theory is defined by Bhander et al. (2003) as the environmentally-conscious design 
process paradox. 
 
==PAGE:34==
Chapter 3. Literature review 
 
18 
 
Figure 3.3: Graphical representation of the environmentally-conscious design process paradox (Bhander et al., 2003). 
 
As presented in Figure 3.3, in each phase the capability to impact positively on the environment 
decreases due to previous made choices which render the subject matter limited to fewer and fewer 
possibilities toward the end of the development. 
 
3.2 LCA and LCC applied to support product development 
 
Numerous are the studies available in the literature where designers and engineers supported the 
product development process with LCA, LCC or both simultaneously in many diverse fields. For 
instance, one recent study conducted by Intrachooto et al. (2017) on the development of Japanese 
Tableware from Palm-Melamine Bio-composites showed how the application of LCA helped 
assessing the various possible compositions of the tableware before the creation of the first 
prototypes. Furthermore, in another recent study, Schöggl et al. (2017) proposed a checklist to 
improve the environmental performances in the early stages of product design in the automotive 
industry and conducted a case study where LCA was applied to assess and quantify the environmental 
benefits of the usage of different materials and support the decision-making process during the 
product design phase. Matos & Hall (2007) emphasized the importance of LCA as optimizing tool 
for product design with two case studies about agricultural biotechnology and oil and sand operations. 
LCA was implemented also by Som et al. (2010) in order to assess the environmental profile of 
innovative nanoproducts, to compare them with their traditional equivalents and to identify 
environmental hotspots within the whole product life cycle. Finally, Vink et al. (2003) studied 
polylactide manufacturing processes, comparing their production with petrochemical-based polymers 
implementing LCA. All those examples are representative of the value of LCA being worldwide 
recognized as a broad applicable and efficient supporting tool during product development. 
 
On the other hand, also LCC has been applied to support decision making and assess various possible 
scenarios and solutions, from an economic point of view, such as assessing the implementation of the 
public transport system in an Australian city (Ally & Pryor, 2016), or evaluating drilling tools in order 
==PAGE:35==
Chapter 3. Literature review 
19
to support decision making in the oil&gas industry (Johannknecht et al.,2016). Another example is 
represented by the application of fibre-reinforced composite materials in the building construction 
sector (Ilg et al., 2016). Even though LCC is still not an international standardized tool, those studies 
generally followed the ISO guidelines dictated for LCA. It is therefore possible to observe the 
common features and structures present in those studies. 
 
There are also case studies where LCA and LCC have been applied simultaneously in order to assess 
both the environmental and economic performances of the systems under study. For instance, Auer 
et al. (2017) proposed an Eco-Care-Matrix where the results from LCA and LCC carried out on two 
different manufacturing systems for glass containers were analysed and compared. Moreover, Aurich 
et al. (2006) proposed a case study on a company producing heavy road construction machines, where 
with a life cycle oriented approach, they tried to optimize and achieve a balance between product 
quality, costs and environmental impacts. Both those studies highlighted how the iterative nature of 
product development, LCA and LCC can be seen as a fully interactive and integrated procedure 
(Nielsen & Wenzel, 2002).  
 
3.3 LCA and LCC applied to insulation products and materials 
 
In the literature available, there are also various life cycle studies applied directly on building 
insulation materials and products, which were analysed to support the choices made during this master 
thesis. Ardente et al. (2008) assessed the environmental benefits and weaknesses of an innovative 
insulation board manufactured in Italy made of natural kenaf fibres, performing a life cycle 
assessment from cradle to grave of the batt. Intini & Kuhtz (2010) carried out a LCA study in order 
to assess the environmental profile of thermal insulation panel made of recycled PET bottles as row 
material. Also, Struhala (2004) and Ricciardini et al. (2014) assessed, using the LCA framework, the 
environmental impacts of new and innovative insulation boards made of recycled materials. The 
former studied panels made of recycled plastic and glass, the latter composed by recycled paper and 
textile fibres. Finally, Schmidt et al. (2004) performed three LCA studies on as many different thermal 
insulation materials (i.e. Stonewool, paper-wool and flax) in order to compare their environmental 
performances, assess the “eco-friendliest”, and study different disposal scenarios.   
 
Also LCC has been applied to assess and support product development of insulation materials and 
products. For instance, Menconi & Grohmann (2014) implemented LCC to evaluate, from an 
economic point of view, roof insulation materials for existing residential constructions. Another 
interesting case study is the one conducted recently by Anastaselos et al. (2017) where he and his 
colleagues investigated the costs of the usage of three different insulation materials, i.e. stone wool, 
expanded polystyrene and extruded polystyrene.  
 
There are also case studies where LCA and LCC have been applied simultaneously in order to asses 
both the environmental and economic performances of insulation products and materials. For 
instance, Ozel et al. (2015) carried out both LCA and LCC in order to optimize the thickness of an 
insulation board considering the economic and environmental costs at the same time. Moreover, 
Lazzarin et al. (2008) performed simultaneously LCA and LCC to evaluate the environmental impacts 
and costs for different insulation solution for a specific case in Italy. 
 
==PAGE:36==
Chapter 3. Literature review 
 
20 
One of the main point in common of the studies listed above, i.e. LCA and/or LCC applied to 
insulations products and materials, is the implementation of the same functional unit. In fact, a 
proposal of the Council for European Producers of Materials for construction (CEPMC) suggested 
the usage of a common functional unit defined as the mass of insulating material which involves a 
thermal resistance R equals to one with a panel with an area of one square meter (CEPMC, 2000). 
The application of a suggested and common functional unit harmonizes and allows the comparison 
between different materials applied as insulants in constructions, focusing only on the environmental 
impacts and insulation properties of the different materials (Ardente et al., 2008). Another shared 
characteristic of the analysed publications is the disregarding of the use phase during the study. 
Schmidt et al. (2004) in his comparative study argued that the use phase for insulation products is 
characterized by an avoidance of impacts due to the increased insulation of buildings. Since the three 
materials under his study (i.e. Stonewool, paper-wool and flax) fulfilled the same functional unit, no 
differences were expected in their environmental impacts during this phase (Schmidt et al., 2004). 
Also, Intini & Kuhtz (2010) in their study disregarded the use phase suggesting that the energy saving 
due to a correct thermal insulation is significantly higher than the impacts caused by the production 
process of the insulation materials. Finally, Ricciardini et al. (2014) did not consider the use phase 
since no data about the environmental impacts were available for their innovative material during this 
stage, being the product still under development. Another interesting point highlighted in the various 
analysed studies is the importance of the disposal phase and especially how waste recycling has the 
potential to lowered the scores upon the final environmental impacts of the insulation products and 
materials (Schmidt et al., 2004; Audenaert et al., 2011). Those insights will be proactively applied 
during the drafting phase of the Goal & Scope definition (Chapter 5) of this thesis, providing strong 
support based on valuable peer-reviewed literature data. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:37==
Chapter 4 
 
Experiments 
 
In the following chapter it is presented and explained in detail the process of the product development 
through different experiments, including an analysis of the various issues raised after each experiment 
and an explanation and justification of the hypothesis tested. It is important to note that the 
development process is not presented in a chronological order, but instead in a logical one. The 
various tests are grouped in eight runs of experiments based on an accurate analysis of the common 
features, which makes the overall process more meaningful and ultimately easier to follow for the 
reader. Finally, after the analysis of the results obtained throughout the tests, four insulation batt 
compositions were identified for the next phase, in this section also the reasoning behind the 
mentioned selection and the factors influencing those choices are presented. In the following phase, 
the four compositions will be assessed from both an economic and environmental point of view, the 
results of the assessments will be used to support and further continue the development process. 
 
It is worth noting that all the materials considered and the choices made during the product 
development process were dictated by the final purpose of the project, meaning creating an insulation 
batt characterized by competitive environmental impacts and by a high eco-friendly profile in 
comparison with the current available alternatives. The choices of the materials applied were also 
influenced by the timeframe of this master thesis project, by the budget and by the availability of 
instruments to run the tests. 
 
4.1 Features of Insulation Batts 
 
Before starting the experiments, it was important to understand which are the main features of the 
commercialized insulation batts and whether there were some characteristics to focus on and 
performances to pursue during the product development process. Of course, the main and necessary 
feature of an insulation batt is to be a good thermal insulant. However, there are other characteristics 
that are also important considering the diverse usages, applications and commercialisations. 
 
4.1.1 General characteristics of insulation batts 
 
The specifications and the general features needed by insulation batts are analysed and listed in the 
following table (Table 4.1) (USDE, 2017; Schmidt et al., 2004; FAO, 2017).  
 
Table 4.1: Specifications and general features of thermal insulation batts (USDE, 2017; Schmidt et al., 2004; FAO, 2017). 
Characteristic 
Comment 
Weight 
The lighter the insulation batt is, the easier is to transport and to handle. 
==PAGE:38==
Chapter 4. Experiments 
 
22
Installation 
The possibility to install the batts easily and rapidly, without the need of taking 
extra effort. 
Application 
versatility 
The possibility of applying the insulation batts as covering on both old and new 
materials. 
Moisture 
The resistance to moisture is important especially if the insulation batt is in 
direct contact with a dump surface (e.g. wall partly below ground). 
Production 
versatility 
The possibility of producing insulation batts with different shapes, curves and 
inclinations.  
Compression 
strength 
This is a required feature when the insulation needs to go beneath structures 
(e.g. roof, floor). 
Sustainability 
The green profile of the products is a characteristic always more appreciated by 
consumers and construction companies.  
Health 
The batt shall not emit toxic substances during its installation and operation. 
Fire resistance 
The fire resistance characteristic is regulated; however higher performances 
might be required in specific applications. 
Acoustic 
insulation 
Sometimes batts can be both thermally and acoustically good insulant. For some 
applications both characteristics are needed. 
Aesthetic 
Aesthetic impact as covering could be a needed feature in some cases. 
 
It is important to note that those are simply general characteristics, and that on the market it is 
impossible to find an insulation batt able to fulfil excellently all those features at the same time. For 
this reason, when builders need to choose which insulation material to implement, they need to make 
a trade-off between the various characteristics and select the most suitable solution for their case and 
mostly within their budget. 
 
4.1.2 Characteristics pursued during the experiments  
 
Considering the current state of the art in insulation batts, and upon consideration of academic 
research and practical application in the construction industry, the experiments tried to reach an ideal 
combination of some of the features listed in Table 4.1 above. For instance, one of the pursued 
characteristic during the product development process was the low density. In fact, it was attempted 
to not alter considerably, with the addition of binders, the standard light weight of paper-wool during 
the composition of the insulation batts. Furthermore, it was also agreed to try to produce flexible 
insulation batts, since they would be easier to fit and adapt during the final installation. Naturally, 
especially from a marketing point of view, also the aesthetic feature was considered during the entire 
product development process. Finally, sustainability was one of the main characteristics desired for 
the insulation batts, in accordance with the objectives of this master thesis project.  
 
On the other hand, the product development process did not consider some of the features listed above 
in Table 4.1 for various reasons. For instance, the experiments did not aim at improving a lower 
resistance to moisture of cellulose fibres and did not take into account the possible acoustic insulation 
capabilities of the thermal insulation batts because outside of the objectives of this master thesis. 
Furthermore, for what concern the health and the fire resistance features, the product development 
process did not focus on these characteristics since they were already certified for the main material 
==PAGE:39==
Chapter 4. Experiments 
 
23
used (i.e. paper-wool) and the binders used were believed to not alter significantly those peculiar 
characteristics.  
 
4.2 Layers approach & Pulp approach 
 
In this section the two approaches, or methods, implemented for the composition of the insulation 
batts, namely “layers approach” and “pulp approach”, are presented. The two approaches were used 
throughout experiments. These two methods were applied to simulate the two most widely used 
manufacturing technologies for cellulose-based boards and insulating batts, i.e. dry process and wet 
process (InTechFibres, 2017). 
 
4.2.1 Layers approach 
 
The layers approach is the first method implemented for the composition of the insulation batts. The 
idea is to build the batts with different layers of paper-wool, with a thickness of approximately one 
centimetre, and to put the binders in between each strata. The batts were composed in an aluminium 
box with a rectangular shape (30x20cm), and starting from the bottom the different layers constituting 
the batt were assembled. The top and bottom strata were always paper-wool layers, for both 
aesthetical and technical reasons. In between each paper-wool layer the binders were applied (e.g. 
glue, wood fibres). The glue was applied either directly or diluted with water and sprayed on the 
paper-wool. Nevertheless, the water usage was low and the required drying time was short, it can be 
considered as a dry assembling process. Figure 4.1 below presents the steps followed for the building 
of the insulation batts during the experiments. 
 
 
Figure 4.1: The main five steps constituting the layers approach. The arrow indicates the need of repetition of the steps 2, 3 and 4 to 
build the different paper-wool layers. This approach was used to perform the first, second third and fourth run of experiments. 
 
It is important to note that from Step 4 the process is repeated twice in order to build different layers 
of paper-wool, and it finally ends with Step 5. During the composition of the layers sometimes there

was the necessity to apply some pressure to compact the paper-wool layer and make it fit the 
rectangular shaped box, increasing in this way the density of the paper-wool and consequently 
decreasing the insulation performances. Nevertheless, throughout the study and during the 
calculations, it is always assumed that the density of the batts is the same as the paper-wool and that 
in an industrialized production of the insulation batts the need of pressure during the manufacturing 
process would not occur. The layers approach was used to perform the first, second, third and fourth 
run of experiments. 
 
4.2.2 Pulp approach 
 
The second method implemented for the composition of the insulation batts is the so-called pulp 
approach. The idea of the method came from the papercrete production, after noticing the tendency 
of paper-wool of sticking together when wetted and then dried. Papercrete is a composite material 
used in the building construction sector. During its composition waste paper is re-pulped and used as 
replacement of part of the Portland cement, having a lower density than traditional concrete. The 
density of papercrete and its mechanical properties can be influenced by the percentage of paper waste 
used during its production (Yun et al., 2007). 
 
During the experiments performed, the applied building process consisted in mixing the paper-wool 
with a high amount of water in order to create a pulp, then, after the addition of additives (e.g. cement), 
the pulp was placed in a shaped container and dried. The pulp was mixed by hand or with the help of 
a mixer (see Appendix A) to get a mixture as homogenous as possible. Oppositely to the layers one, 
the pulp approach can be considered a wet process and consequently the drying time of the insulation 
batts after the manufacturing was extensive. This feature was considered as a drawback of this 
process. The pulp approach was used in the fifth, sixth, seventh and eighth run of experiments. The 
following flow chart (Figure 4.2) summarizes the steps of the described method. 
 
 
 
Figure 4.2: The four Steps constituting the Pulp approach. This method was used to perform the fifth, sixth seventh and eight run of 
experiments. 
STEP 1
STEP 2
STEP 3
STEP 4
THE PAPER IS 
PLACED IN A WOOL 
BATCH AND SOME 
WATER IS ADDED
THE MIXTURE IS 
STIRRED UNTIL IT 
GETS A PULP 
STATE, IF NEEDED 
WATER IS ADDED 
TO WET 
COMPLETELY THE 
PAPER
THE BINDERS 
ARE ADDED (E.G. 
CEMENT), AND 
THE PULP IS 
MIXED
WHEN THE PULP IS

PLACED IN A 
SHAPED 
CONTAINER AND 
DRIED FOR SOME 
DAYS
Pulp approach
==PAGE:41==
