25
4.2.3 Comparison of the two approaches 
 
Different are the characteristics both with advantages and disadvantages of the two implemented 
methods to build the prototypes, those are summarized in Table 4.2 below. 
 
Table 4.2: Different characteristics of the two methods implemented to run the experiments – Pulp approach vs. Layers approach 
 
Layers approach 
Pulp approach 
Homogeneity 
The batt is not homogenous 
The batt is homogenous 
Drying process 
Fast and easy 
Can take up to 7 days 
Replicability 
It depends on the skills of the builder Easy to obtain always the same results 
Water usage 
Low – dry process 
High – wet process 
Need of binding fibres 
Yes 
None 
Flexibility 
Low/medium 
None 
  
As can be seen from Table 4.2 above both approaches have different pros and cons. For instance, the 
time needed for the drying of the batts was one of the main drawback of the pulp approach; it was 
necessary to wait up to seven days in order to obtain insulation batts completely dried and to analyse 
the results. This method gives a more homogenous result increasing the general competitiveness of 
the insulation batt, also from an aesthetic point of view. Furthermore, the pulp approach is easier to 
replicate than the layers approach, since simply by following the formula and using an all-use mixer 
the same results are always obtainable. On the other hand, the latter gives results with a higher 
flexibility than the former, property relevant for the product. In general, there is a trade-off between 
all these advantages and disadvantages, and throughout this study, positively, it will be possible to 
assess in which direction the product development process should continue, taking into account both 
the environmental performances and the production costs. 
 
4.3 First Experiments and results 
 
During the paper recycling process from the C&D waste and the production of paper-wool, it is 
possible to obtain different kind of paper-wool with different sizes. In fact, the different speed of the 
hammer mill (fiberizer) can influence the quality of the paper-wool produced. The first run of 
experiments focused on the three different types of paper-wool obtainable through the manufacturing 
process, i.e. 15 Hz, 20 Hz and 30 Hz paper-wool. The higher is the frequency of the hammer mill the 
finer are the cellulose fibres composing the paper-wool. Primewool Holding Denmark A/S uses the 
30 Hz paper-wool as blowing material because characterized by finer fibres. However, since different 
types of paper-wool are obtainable, the product development process considered also the 
implementation of the other types. The aim of the first experiments was to investigate which paper-
wool type was the most suitable for the insulation batt production and to see the first results of the 
initial approach, i.e. layers approach. The following picture (Figure 4.3) displays the three different 
paper-wool types used to run the experiments. As can be seen in Figure 4.3 the higher the frequency 
of the hammer mill the finer and softer will be the paper-wool obtained. 
==PAGE:42==
Chapter 4. Experiments 
 
26
 
Figure 4.3: Three different paper-wool types obtainable from the manufacturing process, i.e. 30 Hz, 20 Hz, and 10 Hz. The higher is 
the frequency of the hammer mill the finer are the cellulose fibers of the paper-wool. 
 
Apart from the glue, also wood fibres were used as binder for the production of the insulation batt 
prototypes. Two types of wood fibres (spruce) with different thickness were used during the entire 
product development process, i.e. 1 mm and 2 mm (Figure 4.4). The wood fibres were provided by a 
German company specialized in insulation products for thermal, acoustic and fire protection, and 
selected due to their suitable properties. Those fibres are currently used as main component of their 
mineral-bonded wood wool acoustic insulation boards. Their wood fibres were tested both as binder 
and structure for the developed insulation batts for two main reasons. Firstly, being this green material 
already applied in the insulation sector, it was worth testing its performances toward a similar 
application during the experiments (i.e. from main insulation material to binder and structure of the 
insulation material). Furthermore, according to the data available online, those fibres are made by 
wood from sustainable forestry management, characteristic in line with the ideology of the whole 
project (Fibrolith, 2017). 
 
 
Figure 4.4: Spruce Wood fibres used to run the experiments. 1mm thick on the left, 2mm thick on the right. 
==PAGE:43==
Chapter 4. Experiments 
 
27
Seven tests were carried out during the first run of experiments. The materials used to build the 
insulation batts were the following: 
 
• 
Paper-wool: 15 Hz, 20 Hz and 30 Hz 
• 
Wood fibres: 2 mm thick 
• 
Glue for wood and paper 
 
Following the layers approach described, for each batt, different layers of paper-wool, wood fibres 
and glue were created. Firstly, with some pressure the bottom layer was composed in a box, then the 
glue (vinyl acetate based) was applied directly (pure) on the paper-wool and the wood fibres added, 
then again a layer of paper-wool, wood fibres and glue, for three times. Figure 4.5 below shows three 
of the seven batts obtained with the three different types of paper-wool tested. A complete overview 
of the materials used, the experiments run and the results obtained can be found in Appendix A. 
 
 
Figure 4.5: Examples of results with three different types of paper-wool obtained with the first run of experiments. 
 
With the first results, it was possible to draw some considerations on the tests run and on the approach 
used. First of all, it was noticed that with the layers approach all the components were not 
homogenously mixed and it was possible to see the different layers composing the insulation batts 
(see Figure 4.5). It was also noted that a precise application of the glue on the entire surface is required 
since where the glue was not properly applied the paper-wool did not stay together (the insulation 
batt appeared friable). Furthermore, the glue tended to stop on the top of the paper-wool layer not 
==PAGE:44==
Chapter 4. Experiments 
 
28
reaching and covering the entire thickness of the strata. Another issue observed was the lack of 
flexibility of the batt, in fact the glue, when dried, made the insulation batt rigid. Finally, it was 
noticed that the paper-wool and the process was too dry when composing the batt, the fact suggested 
the usage of water to dilute the glue to make the composition process easier. For what concern the 
performances of the three different types of paper-wool tested, since different issues occurred 
concerning the composition, it was not possible to decide and assess already with which type continue 
the experiments. Consequently, also in the second run of experiments all the three different paper-
wool types were used to run the tests. 
 
4.4 Second Experiments and results 
 
Upon consideration of the previous results, five further tests were carried out to build the insulation 
batts. The materials used were the following: 
 
• 
Paper-wool: 15 Hz, 20 Hz and 30 Hz 
• 
Wood fibres: 1mm and 2 mm thick 
• 
Glue for wood and paper 
• 
Water 
 
The method chosen to compose the insulation batts was analogous to the one presented before, i.e. 
layers approach. However, in the second run of experiments the glue was mixed with water and 
distributed with a spray to ensure a more homogenous application. It was also possible to test both 
the types of wood fibres with different thickness (i.e. 1mm and 2mm thick). One of the five tests was 
run without the usage of wood fibres as binder to see the actual need of this component. Figure 4.6 
shows two of the five results obtained. A complete and detailed overview of the materials used, the 
experiments run and the results obtained can be found in Appendix A. 
 
Figure 4.6: Two examples of the results obtained with the second run of experiments. On the left the test run with 2mm wood fibres, 
on the right the test run with 1mm wood fibres. 
==PAGE:45==
Chapter 4. Experiments 
 
29
One of the important findings from those tests was the fundamental role of water, by mixing the glue 
with the latter some considerable advantages were noted. In fact, the mixture of glue and water could 
go through the layer of paper-wool instead of stagnating on the surface, as in the previous 
experiments. Furthermore, the spraying technique was practical and efficient allowing a more 
homogenous spread of the glue on the entire surface of the layer. It was also observed that the water 
had the ability of binding the cellulose fibres together by wetting the paper-wool. However, the glue 
once dried reduced the overall potential flexibility of the insulation batts. For what concern the wood 
fibres, the choice between the two types (i.e. 1mm and 2mm thick) did not influence considerably the 
results. It is worth noting that the sample tested without wood fibres gave an unacceptable result, 
meaning that the wood fibres as binders are needed with this approach. A final consideration was that 
the insulation batt made with 30 Hz paper-wool was more compacted and better aesthetically, since 
the cellulose fibres of this type showed a higher tendency to stay together. For this reason, it was 
decided to focus the following tests only on the 30 Hz paper-wool type. 
 
4.5 Third Experiments and results 
 
During the third run of experiments, considering the previous results, other three tests were carried 
out to build the insulation batts. It is worth highlight that during this run of experiments no glue was 
used and that it was substituted by water alone in order to test the sole role of the latter in binding the 
paper fibres together. The materials used were the following: 
 
• 
Paper-wool: 30 Hz 
• 
Wood fibres: 1mm 
• 
Water 
 
The method used to compose the insulation batts was the layers approach. In this phase of experiments 
only 30 Hz paper-wool was used because, as explained above, in the previous tests it performed better 
than the other two types. The following picture (Figure 4.7) shows one of the results obtained with 
this run of experiments.  
 
 
Figure 4.7: One of the results of the third run of experiment. In this run only water, paper-wool and wood fibres were used. 
==PAGE:46==
Chapter 4. Experiments 
 
30
Having used a larger amount of water the samples needed time to dry, they were left in an oven for 2 
hours at 80°C to improve the drying process (see Figure 4.8). One of the main points of these tests 
was to check the binding properties of water. It was noticed that a more considerable usage of water 
helps the paper to stick together, without the need of any glue, and it helps to obtain a more 
homogenous sample. However, the water alone was not enough as binder. As can be seen in the 
pictures (Figure 4.7) above the surfaces of the batts were not compacted and entire parts of the batt 
left the structure. This fact suggested that glue is needed to bind the wood fibres (structure) to the 
paper and to keep the insulation batt together. A complete and detailed overview of the materials 
used, the experiments run and the results obtained can be found in Appendix A. 
 
 
Figure 4.8: Picture of the oven used to dry the insulation batts at 80°C during the tests. 
 
4.6 Fourth Experiments and results 
 
Considering the previous results, seven tests were carried out to build the insulation batts, during the 
fourth run of experiments. New components were tested to overcome the two main issues observed: 
low flexibility and friability of the batts. The new binders tested were natural kenaf fibres instead of 
wood fibres, to reduce the rigidity of the insulation batt, and an innovative natural glue made of 
cellulose ether powder dissolved and mixed in water.  
 
Kenaf (Hibiscus cannabinus) is an herbaceous annual plant belonging to the family of Malvaceae. 
The fibres in the kenaf plant can be found in the bast (bark) and in the core (wood). Kenaf is cultivated 
widely around the world (e.g. USA, India, Indonesia, South Africa and south Europe) since it needs 
few treatments during the cultivation and it growths in a wide range of weathers. Different are the 
applications of this raw material such as alternative of wood in the pulp and paper industries, 
insulation, animal bedding and packing material (Sellers, 1999; Ardente et al., 2008; KEFI, 2017; 
Amaducci, 2000). 
 
The natural kenaf fibres used to run the experiments were provided by an Italian company specialized 
in the production of natural-fibres products for automotive components, soundproofing systems, 
thermal insulation and green building. In their production of the insulation batts, the Italian company 
==PAGE:47==
Chapter 4. Experiments 
 
31
uses the kenaf fibres as main constituent instead of binder which would be the application during the 
experiments. Again, the choice of this material as binder was influenced by the eco-friendly profile 
of the kenaf fibres studied and assessed by Ardente et al. (2008). The kenaf fibres were delivered as 
twists and for the tests they were unfolded and cut manually as shown in Figure 4.9.  
 
 
Figure 4.9: Natural kenaf fibers before and after the unfolding of the twist and the cutting process. 
   
Cellulose ethers are polymers manufactured by a chemical variation of cellulose, they are applied in 
different industries and sectors (e.g. food, pharmaceutical and personal care products) with different 
purposes such as thickening, binding and water-retention agents (HIS Markit, 2016). The cellulose 
ether used during the tests was carboxymethylcellulose (CMC) and was delivered by a Chinese 
company in form of white powder. It was necessary to dissolve completely the cellulose ether in 
water (1% solution) for 3-4 hours to obtain the desired consistency of the glue. 
 
The material used for the fourth run of experiments were the following: 
 
• 
Paper-wool: 30 Hz 
• 
Wood fibres: 1mm 
• 
Water 
• 
Cellulose ether powder 
• 
Kenaf fibres 
 
The method used to compose the insulation batts was the layers approach. Again, only 30 Hz paper-
wool was utilized during these tests because it performed better than the other two types, i.e. 15 Hz 
and 20 Hz. The following pictures (Figure 4.10) present two of the seven results obtained. A complete 
and detailed overview of the materials used, the experiments run and the results obtained can be found 
in Appendix A.  
 
==PAGE:48==
Chapter 4. Experiments 
 
32
 
Figure 4.10: Two of the results obtained with the fourth run of experiments. As binder, for the batt on the left wood fibers were used, 
for the one on the right kenaf fibers were used instead. 
 
Starting from the fibres, the kenaf fibres performed well in comparison to the wood fibres for what 
concern the rigidity of the insulation batt. In fact, when trying to bend or warp the batts, the kenaf 
fibres gave a much higher flexibility to the product. Regarding the binding performances both fibres 
performed similarly, helping the paper-wool to stay together and forming the structure of the 
insulation batt. The glue obtained from the dissolution of the cellulose ether powder into water 
performed well but it decreased the flexibility of the batt, in fact when dried the insulation batt remain 
rigid. As can be seen in the Figure 4.10 above on the right, when dried the glue gave a solid 
consistency to the paper-wool forming a hard layer. The fact suggested to test again the cellulose 
ether glue but with a lower concentration in water. In fact, apart from this drawback, in general it 
performed better than the one used in the previous experiments. Again, since the higher amount of 
water used, the insulation batts needed some hours to dry, to make the process faster and easier they 
were dried in oven at 80°C for a couple of hours. 
 
After all these different combinations of compositions tested two general main issues remained 
unsolved. Firstly, the layers approach do not give homogenous results, the different layers were still 
visible and not perfectly equals, since the building was hand-made. Secondly, in the case water and 
glue were not applied perfectly, parts remained unbended to the rest of the batt. In order to overcome 
this issue a new method of composition was implemented in the following tests, i.e. pulp approach. 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:49==
Chapter 4. Experiments 
 
33
4.7 Fifth Experiments and results 
 
With the fifth run of experiments, for the reasons explained above, it was agreed to change the 
building method from the layers approach to the pulp approach. Inspired by the production of 
papercrete it was decided to try to produce a paper pulp with a high amount of water and then add a 
binder, with the fibres, such as cement. The materials used to run these tests were the following: 
 
• 
Paper-wool: 30 Hz 
• 
Wood fibres: 1 mm 
• 
Kenaf fibres 
• 
Cement 
• 
Water 
 
Five tests were run with different combinations and ratios. In comparison with the general formula to 
produce papercrete, much less cement was used to prevent a severe increase in the density of the 
product. Figure 4.11 below presents two of the obtained results. A complete and detailed overview 
of the materials used, the experiments run and the results obtained can be found in Appendix A. 
 
 
Figure 4.11: Two of the obtained results of the fifth run of experiments. As binder, for the batt on the left wood fibers were used, for 
the one on the right kenaf fibers were used instead 
 
As can be seen from the Figure 4.11 above with this new approach it was possible to obtain a much 
more uniform result, increasing also the aesthetic aspect of the insulation batt. The usage of the 
cement as binder gives to the batts a much higher strength and resistance. However, once combined 
with the cement, the paper-wool lost completely its characteristic softness, flexibility and mobility. 
From the results, it was also possible to note that, contrarily from the previous experiments, no 
==PAGE:50==
Chapter 4. Experiments 
 
34
differences were evident in the usage of the two types of binding fibres, i.e. wood fibres and kenaf 
fibres.  
 
In the same run of experiments, samples were composed without the usage of binding fibres, in order 
to test the actual need of those with the new approach. The tests were run using only paper-wool, 
water and cement. Figure 4.12 below presents three of the results obtained without the usage of 
binding fibres. As before, a complete and detailed overview of the materials used, the experiments 
run and the results obtained can be found in Appendix A. 
 
  
 
Figure 4.12: Three results of the tests run without the usage of binding fibers. For all the three samples, only cement was used as 
binder with different paper-wool/cement ratios. The sample on top-left has a ratio of 2:1, the one on the bottom-left corner a ratio of 
5:4, and the one of the left a ratio of 3:1. 
 
As can be seen in the pictures above (Figure 4.12), the absence of the fibres gives a smoother surface 
to the batts, better from an aesthetic point of view. Moreover, due to the high performance of the 
cement as a binder it seemed that there was no need of binding fibres to keep together the paper-wool. 
In general, one of the first noted drawbacks of this approach is the long drying process, it took from 
three to seven days to dry completely the insulation batts (no oven was used). From a manufacturing 
prospective, the drying time would be a tough issue to tackle in a big scale production. On the other 
hand, if cement is used as binder, this time frame is the minimum awaiting time for a proper 
strengthening of the latter. 
 
==PAGE:51==
Chapter 4. Experiments 
 
35
4.8 Sixth Experiments and results 
 
After the first run of experiments with the pulp approach it was noticed that the water played a 
fundamental role in the new method, for this reason it was decided to investigate the right amount of 
water to implement during the pulp production. The material used for the sixth run of experiments 
were the following: 
 
• 
Paper-wool: 30 Hz 
• 
Water 
• 
Cement 
• 
Recycled Gypsum 
 
Four experiments were run with the same quantity of paper-wool but with different percentages of 
water, and cement. Differently from the previous experiments, for this run, the shape of the container 
was a cube with the side of 5 cm to make the comparison of the samples easier and more practical. 
Figure 4.13 below summarizes the results. A complete and detailed overview of the materials used, 
the experiments run and the results obtained can be found in Appendix A. 
 
 
Figure 4.13: Four results of the sixth run of experiments. The four samples were composed with the same amount of paper-wool but 
with a decreasing usage of water from the left. The binder used for these samples is cement. 
 
As can be seen from the picture above, even though the amount of paper-wool is the same, the volume 
of each sample increase with the amount of water implemented. The higher is the amount of water 
used the higher is the volume of the sample. One possible explanation of this phenomenon is that the 
water when evaporating from the sample leaves room for air in the paper giving a higher volume to 
the sample. It is important to note that the amount of water tested was influenced by the fact that to 
obtain a wet pulp all the paper-wool fibres need to be wetted by water (i.e. a minimum amount 
needed). 
 
In order to investigate further the role of water and the more suitable ratio between water and paper-
wool, other experiments were run with the same amount of paper-wool and different quantitative of 
water. The same container as before was used, i.e. a cube with a side of 5 cm. Moreover, in this run, 
a new possible binder was tested with the cement, i.e. recycled gypsum. The choice was influenced 
by the fact that the recycled gypsum is one by-product of the recycling units before the manufacturing 
==PAGE:52==
Chapter 4. Experiments 
 
36
process of paper-wool from the plasterboard waste. Moreover activated gypsum is known and used 
in the building construction sector as a binder, for this reason the recycled fraction of gypsum was 
tested. The following picture (Figure 4.14) shows the results. A complete and detailed overview of 
the materials used, the experiments run and the results obtained can be found in Appendix A. 
 
 
Figure 4.14: Four example of the sixth run of experiments. The four samples were composed with the same amount of paper-wool 
but with a decreasing amount of water from the left. In this run the binder used was a mixture of cement and recycled gypsum. 
 
As for the previous test, it can be seen from Figure 4.14 that the higher the amount of water used the 
higher the volume of the dried sample (the amount of water used decrease from the left to the right 
in Figure 4.14). For what concerns the recycled gypsum as binder, from the results it was not possible 
to see a difference with the previous tests, however it is important to note that also cement was used 
in the mixture as binder. The fact suggested two possibilities. The first one is that, assuming no 
binding properties of recycled gypsum, it was possible to use a lower amount of cement as binder and 
obtain good results. The second one, assuming binding properties of the recycled gypsum, is that also 
this material has good binding properties and was contributing to the results. To answer those 
hypothesis, tests were run using only recycled gypsum as binder. From the results it was assessed that 
recycled gypsum has no binding properties for the paper-wool, thus the first hypothesis was 
confirmed, meaning that it was possible to use less cement to bind the paper-wool and obtain good 
results. In the Appendix A it is also possible to find a complete overview of the results obtained using 
only recycled gypsum as binder.  
 
4.9 Seventh Experiments and results 
 
In the next run of experiments the cellulose ether powder was tested as binder with the new building 
method, i.e. pulp approach. The cellulose ether was dissolved in water with a concentration of one 
per cent. For these experiments, also the wood and kenaf fibres were implemented. The materials 
used for the seventh run of experiments were the following: 
 
• 
Paper-wool: 30 Hz 
• 
Wood fibres: 1mm 
• 
Water 
• 
Cellulose ether powder 
==PAGE:53==
Chapter 4. Experiments 
 
37
• 
Kenaf fibres 
 
Figure 4.15 below shows two results of the seven tests run. A complete and detailed overview of the 
materials used, the experiments run and the results obtained can be found in Appendix A. 
 
Figure 4.15: Two examples of the seven results during the seventh run of experiments. The batts are composed by paper-wool 30 Hz, 
kenaf fibres and cellulose ether dissolved in water as binder. As binding fibres, for the batt on the left wood fibres were used, for the 
batt on the right kenaf fibres were used instead. 
 
First of all, as can be seen from Figure 4.15 above, differently from the previous tests run with cement, 
the mixture of glue and water did not give to the insulation batt a smooth and homogenous exterior, 
but a rough and incongruous surface. Moreover, it is also possible to see some cracks on the surface 
from the exterior. One advantage of the usage of cellulose ether glue instead of cement is the lower 
weight of the obtained insulation batts. In fact, the weight of the dried sample is almost equal to the 
amount of paper used, since all the water evaporated during the drying process (see Appendix A). 
However, it was noticed that the pulp production process was more difficult with the water mixed 
with the cellulose ether. Considering the results obtained it was decided that for the pulp approach, it 
was not worth considering any further cellulose ether as binder. 
 
4.10 Eighth Experiments and results 
 
In order to evaluate another binder with the pulp approach, other three tests were run using activated 
gypsum instead of cement as binder. The choice came from the properties of the activated gypsum. 
In fact, it is used as a binder in the construction sector and it is lighter then cement. The material used 
for the eighth run of experiments are listed below: 
 
• 
Paper-wool: 30 Hz 
• 
Water 
• 
Activated Gypsum 
==PAGE:54==
Chapter 4. Experiments 
 
38
The procedure followed for the composition of the samples was the same as before when the cement 
was implemented, i.e. pulp approach. The following picture (Figure 4.16) presents three results 
obtained with the eighth run of experiments. As before, a complete and detailed overview of the 
amounts of materials used, the experiments run and the results obtained can be found in Appendix A. 
 
 
Figure 4.16: Three results obtained during the eighth run of experiments. The samples were obtained using water, paper-wool 30 Hz 
and activated gypsum as binder. 
 
As can be seen from Figure 4.16 above also the activated gypsum, as the cement, gives smooth surface 
to the sample and works properly as binder. One advantage in comparison with the cement is the 
lower specific weight of the activated gypsum, interesting feature in order to make the insulation batts 
lighter. One drawback is the strength, to get results as strong as with the cement much more gypsum 
would be needed. However, the batts are not supposed to reinforce structures so the strength is not 
one of the main properties to keep in consideration. Also in this run of experiments the drying time 
needed was between 3 and 7 days. 
 
4.11 Conclusion of the experiments 
 
From all the presented experiments, it is clear that the two different compositing methods (i.e. layer 
and pulp approach) gave different results with different characteristics, advantages and disadvantages 
to consider for the further product development process. Table 4.3 below summarizes all the main 
points of each run of experiments, underlining the hypothesis tested, the results and the findings. The 
table enables the reader to understand the decisions laying behind the choice of the four ideal 
compositions to model and assess from an economic and environmental point of view.

Chapter 4. Experiments 
 
42
Upon analysis of all the experiments and the presented results, four different compositions were 
chosen for the modelling part. With four ideal compositions, it was possible to narrow down the 
overall findings of the previous experiments, providing a compelling ground for the assessment. In 
this way, it was possible to critically determine in which direction the product development process 
should further continue, considering together with the technical and aesthetical factors also the 
economic and environmental performances. 
 
It was decided that of the four selected compositions, two were produced with the layers approach 
and two with the pulp approach, since both approaches gave good results with different advantages 
and drawbacks. The common point in all the four compositions selected is the implementation of the 
30 Hz paper-wool as suggested by the results of the second run of experiments. 
 
Starting from the first two compositions with the layers approach, it was clear from the results of the 
third run of experiments that the presence of the binding fibres for the structure of the batt was needed, 
i.e. wood and kenaf fibres. It was not possible to assess which one performed better so the wood 
fibres will be considered for the first composition to assess and the kenaf for the second one. 
Moreover, during the first and second run of experiments it was observed the need of some water to 
help the binding, for this reason in both compositions some water will be considered. As explained 
above, both tested glues (i.e. vinyl acetated based glue and cellulose ether powder) performed well 
with some advantages and disadvantages, for this reason both will be studied in the next phase. 
 
For what concern the two compositions with the pulp approach, from the fifth run of experiments it 
was noted that there was no need of binding fibres, consequently for these insulation batts no fibres 
will be implemented. Both the tested binders (i.e. cement and activated gypsum) performed well so 
both will be considered in the two pulp approach combinations. Table 4.4 below summarizes the 
combinations of the four described compositions. 
 
Table 4.4: The materials of the four ideal compositions considered for the environmental and economic modelling investigation.

In conclusion all the experiments were needed and fundamental to formulate the presented four 
compositions. The idea is that after the economic and environmental assessment the product 
development will continue further using the results of the two assessments as supporting tools. It is 
worth noting that other possible compositions (e.g. layers approach with cellulose ether with wood 
==PAGE:59==

fibres) will not be modelled. But from the obtained results it will be possible also to assess the single 
components, enabling a more wide and general assessment of the various possibilities.

Chapter 5 
 
Goal & Scope Definition 
 
5.1 Goal Definition 
 
5.1.1 Intended application of the results 
 
This study is part of a master thesis project conducted in collaboration between Danmarks Tekniske 
Universitet (DTU) and Primewool Holding Denmark A/S. The intended application of the study is to 
investigate and evaluate from an environmental and economic point of view the improvement 
potentials from changes in the product design, analysing the possible “what if” scenarios raised during 
the product development process. Moreover, the identification of the parts of the product contributing 
with the greatest share on the overall environmental impacts will give insights and suggestions 
regarding the directions to follow during the further product development process. The results are 
intended to provide a deeper knowledge on the sustainability and possibility of development of this 
new product. Both the LCA and LCC results are intended for internal use. 
 
5.1.2 Limitations due to methodological choices 
 
First of all, during this project LCA and LCC studies will be carried out considering only 
environmental and economic criteria. Consequently, no assessment on societal impacts will be 
performed, disregarding completely the societal criteria from the final results and conclusions. 
 
As explained in the previous section (Literature review – Chapter 3) the use phase will be disregarded 
in this study and consequently in the results the impacts related to this phase will not be considered. 
In accordance with Schmidt et al. (2004) this simplification is justified by the fact that all the possible 
compositions studied, fulfilling the same functional unit, will have the same environmental impacts 
during this phase. The focus will be on the composition of the insulation batts, the manufacturing 
process and on their disposal. 
 
There are limitations in the use of the results, since they are intended only for internal use and not to 
be used for comparative assertion. 
 
5.1.3 Decision-context and reason for carrying out the study 
 
This is a product related decision support study on product development level, since the insulation 
batts are still under development and not yet produced. The decision made do not influence the 
available production capacity or cause large scale consequences in the background systems. 
Therefore, the chosen decision context is situation A, a micro-level decision support context 
(European Commission, 2010).  
==PAGE:62==
 Chapter 5. Goal & Scope Definition 
 
46 
The obtained results are intended to be used by Primewool Holding Denmark A/S (i.e. the 
commissioner) and their research and development department as a decision support tool for further 
investments and to assess the environmental impacts and costs of their product under development. 
 
5.1.4 Target audience 
 
The target audience of this study are the commissioner itself Primewool Holding Denmark A/S, its 
research and development (R&D) department, who have provided the data and who will be among 
the main users of the results, and the department of management engineering at DTU. 
 
5.1.5 Comparative studies to be disclosed to the public/Type of Assessment 
 
The study is of comparative nature since different scenarios and compositions of the product are 
evaluated and compared. It is not intended to be disclosed to the public, consequently an external 
review process will not be needed. 
 
5.1.6 Commissioner of the study and other influential actors 
 
The commissioner of this study is Primewool Holding Denmark A/S and its research and 
development department. 
 
 
5.2 Scope Definition 
 
5.2.1 Deliverables 
 
This study is a comparative LCA combined with an LCC. It provides a detailed LCI model of the 
different scenarios and systems of interest. The report includes a presentation of the systems 
investigated, the interpretation of the results, discussions and conclusions and it also considers the 
completeness and validity of the different compositions of paper-wool insulation batts studied. Also, 
the superiority, equality or inferiority between the compared scenarios is investigated. By comparing 
the different scenarios, weak points are identified to improve their specific performances. Finally, the 
study provides a decision-support tool for the further development of the product. 
 
5.2.2 Object of the assessment 
 
Function 
 
The goal of the LCA and of the LCC studies is to compare and assess different possibilities for the 
composition of the studied insulation batts. Each different batt needs to fulfil the traditional 
requirements of insulation materials as listed in the obligatory properties below (Table 5.1). In order 
to formulate the functional unit, the obligatory and positioning properties are considered and listed 
below in Table 5.1.  
==PAGE:63==
Chapter 5. Goal & Scope Definition 
 
 
47
Table 5.1: List of the obligatory properties and of the positioning properties for thermal insulation batts (Schmidt et al., 2004). 
Obligatory properties 
Positioning properties 
 The thermal insulation batt must fit and 
fill out the construction area avoiding air 
spaces. 
 
 The insulation batt shall be unchanged to 
moisture and resistant to biological 
assault (decomposition, mould).

should be in line with the actual 
regulations. 
 Sustainability 
 
 Easy to work with and to install 
 
 No allergic reactions to the workers 
during the installation procedures. 
 
Functional Unit 
 
In accordance with a proposal of Council for European Producers of Materials for construction 
(CEPMC) the functional unit is defined as the mass of insulating batts which involves a thermal 
resistance R equals to 1 with a panel with an area of 1 m2 (CEPMC, 2000): 
 
𝑓. 𝑢. = 𝑅∗𝜆∗𝜌∗𝐴 
 
Where R is the thermal resistance in m2K/W,  is the thermal conductivity measured as W/(m K),   
the density of the insulation product in kg/m3 and A is the area of the batts and equals 1 m2. CEPMC 
suggested 50 years as use phase. Moreover, the same unit was used in the criteria for EU eco-labelling 
of insulation materials (Ardente et al., 2008). It is important to note that, such a functional unit gives 
information about the amount of insulation material required to perform a given thermal resistance 
during the insulation lifetime, focusing only on the environmental and insulating properties of the 
assessed material (Ardente et al., 2008). 
 
Reference flows 
 
Respecting the functional unit, the reference flows were calculated in order to perform the study. 
Tests conducted by Dansk Teknologisk Institut shows that the insulation capacity of Primewool’s 
paper-wool corresponds to a  equal to 0,038 W/(m K) for insulation of attics and 0,037 W/(m K)  
for insulation of walls (Primewool, 2017). In this study, the thermal conductivity of the paper-wool 
is considered equal to 0,0375 W/(m K) while the density of the product equal to 28,5 kg/m3 
(Primewool, 2017). Applying the equation above it was possible to calculate that 1,07 kilograms of 
paper-wool are needed to fulfil the functional unit. Table 5.2 below summarizes the parameters used 
for the calculation of the reference flow. 
==PAGE:64==
 Chapter 5. Goal & Scope Definition 
 
48

It is important to note that for all the tested scenarios the same reference flows for paper-wool were 
applied since paper-wool is the main material and it is assumed that the other components are not 
influencing significantly the density and the insulation performances of the insulation batt. 
 
5.2.3 System boundaries and completeness requirements 
 
The boundaries of the studied system are set equals for all the different composition scenarios 
analysed. In 2012, the life cycle stages of construction products have been harmonised by the 
European standard EN15804:2012 (Pargana et al., 2014). Three different life cycle boundaries have 
been established, i.e. from Cradle to Gate, from Cradle to Gate with options and from Cradle to Grave.

Following the standards summarized in the table above, a life cycle study on building products can 
be defined from “Cradle to Gate” when it is covering only the product stage (A1-A3). It is defined 
“Cradle to Gate with options” when it is covering the product stage (A1-A3) and at least another stage 
of the life cycle. In this kind of study all the other stages are optional and their implementation 
depends on the objectives of the study. A study defined as “Cradle to Gate” covers all the stages listed 
in the table above (A1-D). 
 
In this study a life cycle from “Cradle to Gate with options” is performed, as explained above the use 
stage it is disregarded and only the disposal after the production is considered. The study covers the 
recycling of the paper from the Plasterboard waste, its refining into paper-wool, the production of the 
insulation batts and their final disposal. The boundaries and the phases of the studied systems are 
graphically presented in the following diagram (Figure 5.1).

overview and description of the processes included in all the different insulation batt compositions

5.2.4 LCI modelling framework and handling of multifunctional processes 
 
In accordance with the decision-context chosen, the attributional LCI modelling principle is applied 
to the study, since the analysed and possible implemented systems are inserted into a static 
technosphere (i.e. steady-state). Furthermore, in this way, it is possible to obtain a description of the 
environmental impacts and of the costs of the products as they are (European Commission, 2010). 
 
Considering the described system multifunctional processes arise. Only for the process “Plasterboard 
Waste Recycling & Refining” multi-functionality needs to be considered. In fact, during this process, 
both paper and gypsum are recycled. Thus, to handle this multi-functionality and to consider both 
products, allocation is performed using physical relationships. To partition the amount of inputs and 
outputs of the two products, mass balance is used as allocation key. 
 
5.2.5 Representativeness of LCI data 
 
Geographical representativeness  
 
Since the plasterboard and paper recycling facilities are located in Denmark and also would be the 
possible future implementations to produce the insulation batts, all the processes studied and 
modelled in this study are intended to be representative for this country. 
 
Time-related representativeness  
 
The time representativeness of the study is the present since the manufacturing process of the 
insulation batts is not already implemented. However, it is noted that the Plasterboard waste recycling 
and refining unit was built in 2008 and that the manufacturing of paper-wool process was installed in 
2015. 
 
Technological representativeness  
 
The technological representativeness for the study is assumed to be, as the time-related, the present 
technology level for the production of the paper-wool and also for the future implementations to 
produce the insulation batts. 
 
5.2.6 Preparation of the basis for the impact assessment 
 
Following the European guidelines of EN15804:2012 on construction products, the environmental 
impact assessment should involve at least seven midpoint impact categories: global warming, ozone 
depletion, soil and water acidification, eutrophication, photochemical ozone creation and depletion 
of abiotic resources. The environmental impact assessment method (EIAM) chosen to characterizes 
the impacts of the thermal insulation batts under study is “ReCiPe 2008 (H)”. This method has been 
chosen for its regional validity for Europe (Menoufi, 2011), and the regional prospective for default 
model (ReCiPe, 2017). Moreover, it allows also the characterizations of the environmental impacts 
also at endpoint level. There are advantages and disadvantages when using endpoint impact categories 
==PAGE:67==
Chapter 5. Goal & Scope Definition 
 
 
51
instead of midpoint. For instance, endpoint results are easier to communicate, interpret and have a 
higher environmental relevance thanks to their form. On the other hand, endpoints categories have 
higher modelling uncertainties compared to the midpoint ones due to the additional assumptions and 
to the value choices made (Laurent, 2016). For those reasons in the LCIA section the results will be 
calculated and presented at both midpoint and endpoint level. “ReCiPe 2008 Midpoint (H)” will be 
implemented to characterize the environmental impacts of the products under study at midpoint level. 
Table 5.4 below presents the midpoint impact categories, with their units and abbreviations, 
considered in this study.

In order to compare the different insulation batt compositions analyzed within impact categories and 
to express and communicate the results on a common scale both internal and external normalization 
will be performed. The division by maximum approach is chosen for the internal normalization, while 
factors provided by the EIAM will be implemented for the external normalization (ReCiPe, 2017).  
 
5.2.7 Special requirements for system comparisons 
 
The present LCA study is a comparative study and consequently, in accordance with the ILCD 
guidelines, the same functional unit is considered for all the different insulation batt compositions 
studied. Furthermore, equivalent methodological considerations in terms of performance, data 
quality, allocation procedures, decision rules on evaluating inputs, outputs and impact assessment 
method, are applied.  
 
5.2.8 Needs for critical review 
 
Since the results of both the LCA and LCC study are not intended to be published, in accordance with 
the ISO standards there is no need for external critical review, however since the report is part of a 
master thesis project it will be evaluated by examiners and censor. 
 
5.2.9 Planning reporting of results 
 
The form of the reporting will be a detailed report. The calculations, methodologies and assumptions 
implemented will be justified and described; also, the sources and the representativeness of the 
gathered data will be indicated. As defined in the goal definition the LCA and LCC are carried out 
for internal use, hence the level of reporting will be “internal” as defined by the ILCD Handbook 
(European Commission, 2010).  
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:69==
Chapter 6 
 
Life Cycle Inventory Analysis 
 
In the following section, firstly the planning of the collection of the data and a general flow diagram 
valid for the four batt compositions are presented. The unit processes of the different systems are 
identified, quantified and described in detail. Furthermore, the quality of the inventory data is 
assessed and the assumptions made to build the models are presented and justified. 
 
 
6.1 Planning data collection  
 
In every study, in order to obtain and deliver consistent and reliable results high quality data are 
fundamental. During the ‘planning data collection’ phase, in order to analyse in detail the data 
involved and needed, the entire production process of the insulation batts has been divided in three 
stages: plasterboard waste recycling, manufacturing of paper-wool and insulation batts 
manufacturing. It is important to note that in case a process highly influence the results the quality of 
the data used is required to be high due to their relevance. Moreover, planning and data collection of 
an LCA study are two iterative processes. In fact both these procedures are integrated parts of the 
iterative approach to LCA (European Commission, 2010). Many are the factors influencing the 
quality of the obtainable data (e.g. time, availability, budget). A useful tool to investigate and 
understand which data influence the final results and consequently are more relevant for the study is 
the sensitivity analysis. Table 6.1 below summarizes the three stages in which the entire system has 
been divided for the planning data collection and presents the data intended to collect for each stage.

6.2 Flow Diagrams and Construction of the unit processes 
 
In this study four different insulation batts compositions are assessed, consequently four different 
flow diagrams are studied and presented. It is important to note that since all the four insulation batts 
are made with the same main material (i.e. paper-wool) some units of the production process are 
shared. The following scheme (Figure 6.1) presents a general flow diagram valid for all the four 
insulation batt systems. The dashed red frame marks the boundary between the Ecosphere and the 
Technosphere, while the continue black frame divides the foreground and the background system. 
All the elementary flows included in the foreground system are modelled from scratch as they are not 
available in the databases libraries of SimaPro, whilst the ones outside the Foreground system are 
modelled with processes already present in the databases of the program. Note that later in this section 
each insulation batt composition, with its own flow diagram, will be presented individually and in 
detail. 
 
 
Figure 6.1: General flow diagram valid for the four different insulation batts compositions analysed and studied during this study. The 
red dashed framework marks the boundary between Ecosphere and Technosphere, while the black continue framework divides the 
Background system to the Foreground system. 
 
Before the analysis of the peculiarities of each ideal composition, the shared unit processes will be 
presented, i.e. the sequence of processes used to produce paper-wool. The two process units in 
common are the “Plasterboard waste Recycling & Refining” unit and the “Paper recycling unit (PRU) 
and Manufacturing of the paper-wool” unit. In the former the paper-based C&D waste is treated and

6.3 Plasterboard waste Recycling & Refining 
 
The first unit process of the entire assessed system is the Plasterboard waste Recycling & Refining 
unit. Here the paper-based C&D waste starts its recycling process. The gypsum fraction is separated 
from the rest of the waste and it is devoted to the manufacturing of new plasterboard. In this unit, also 
the ferrous contaminants are sorted from the paper-based waste through a magnetic separator. The 
following picture (Figure 6.2) displays part of the analysed unit. 
 
 
 
Figure 6.2: Picture of part of the Plasterboard waste Recycling & Refining unit assessed during this study.

compositions assessed, since this unit is shared and precede the paper-wool production process. A 
complete overview of the single data used to assess the environmental impacts in SimaPro can be 
found in Appendix B.

The output of this process is composed by paper, still highly contaminated by gypsum (more than 
50% of the total weight), and other fractions of materials such as plastic and wood. For this reason, 
another sorting step is necessary in order to clean the paper fraction and prepare it for the 
manufacturing of paper-wool.  
 
6.4 Paper recycling unit (PRU) and Manufacturing of the paper-wool 
 
The contaminated paper enters the PRU and Manufacturing unit directly from the previous unit since 
the two processes are built in succession. In this unit all the remaining contaminants are sorted from 
the paper fraction (i.e. plastic, wood and non-ferrous metals) and another high amount of gypsum is 
recovered, then the paper is mixed in a fiberization process with a flame retardant (MAP-
monoammonium phosphate, 7.5% w/w) and a fungicide (IPBC - Iodopropynyl Butyl Carbamate, 
0.1% w/w) in order to produce the wanted paper-wool. The following picture (Figure 6.3) displays 
part of this unit process in which the cellulose based blowing insulant is produced. 
 
 
Figure 6.3: Picture of Paper recycling unit (PRU) and Manufacturing of the paper-wool unit assessed during this study. 
==PAGE:73==
Chapter 6. Life Cycle Inventory 
 
57
The technical challenge has been to clean the paper from the contaminants, among others, from the 
construction product that the paper was surrounded. Previously this fact prevented the paper from 
being recycled. In order to achieve an optimal rate of sorting and recycling, innovative and patented-
pending processes have been invented and are now assuring that the resulting paper can effectively 
be used as an insulation product. Table 6.3 below summarizes the composition of the final paper-
wool ready for the insulation batts production, after the addition of the fungicide (IPBC) and the 
flame retardant (MAP) to the cellulose fibres.

6.8 Disposal & Transport 
 
In accordance with the Goal and Scope definition of this study, also the disposal stage is considered. 
Since the project is located in Denmark, the most likely disposal method is assumed to be incineration. 
Obviously, the four insulation batts are not present in the databases available in SimaPro, 
consequently, since paper-wool is the main component of the insulation batts under study, in the 
model this process is simplified as waste paperboard to municipal incineration. 
 
For what concern the transport, when it is not already included in the implemented databases of 
SimaPro, this factor in the four models is generally disregarded for two main reasons. Firstly, for the 
lack of data availability and secondly because all the four different compositions would share the 
same transport distances for the different processes. For instance, in the disposal stage all the four 
different scenarios would involve the same distances from the installation place to the municipal 
incineration. Furthermore, the first three unit processes would be implemented in series (the first two 
are already in series), this fact would bring the transport in between the processes to zero. 
Nevertheless, since this study focus on the environmental impacts due to the different possible 
compositions of the insulation batts it was decided to consider the transport for the materials used in 
the batts manufacturing process (i.e. binders and fibres). For instance, the wood fibres were delivered 
from a German company, the kenaf fibres from an Italian one and the cellulose ether glue directly 
from China. In Appendix B it is possible to find in detail the calculations and the assumptions made 
to model the transport of the materials involved in the manufacturing of the insulation batts under 
study. 
 
6.9 Main assumption for modelling 
 
Due to the lack of information, time and budget constraints, some assumptions were necessary during 
the building of the life cycle inventory model. The main assumptions made during this phase are 
summarized in this section. A complete list of the assumptions made for the building of each unit and 
the entire model can be found in Appendix B. 
 
All the four ideal batt compositions, fulfilling the same functional unit, are made with the same 
amount of paper-wool. Through the study it is assumed that the only material contributing to the batts 
insulation properties is paper-wool and that the addition of binders is not going to interfere with its 
insulation performances. Furthermore, even though, two compositions are made with the layers 
approach (dry process) and two with the pulp approach (wet process) both are fulfilling the functional 
unit with the same amount of insulation material. 
 
The quality of the waste treated can vary a lot and consequently also the outputs of the first two units. 
Average values have been provided by Primewool and have been used in order to create an average 
scenario in the model, it is noted that the amounts of waste outputs (e.g. metals, wood) can vary a lot 
==PAGE:79==
Chapter 6. Life Cycle Inventory 
 
63
with the quality of the C&D waste treated and also with its water content (completely dry waste vs. 
wet waste).  
 
In this study the input of C&D waste (i.e. plasterboard waste) in the Plasterboard waste Recycling 
and Refining unit is modelled as burden free (zero burden). Furthermore, in the same unit, allocation 
is performed in order to assign a proportion of the environmental impacts of the product system under 
study. In fact, since both outputs from this unit (i.e. contaminated paper and recycled gypsum) have 
relevant value, it was decided to perform mass allocation in order to express the physical relation 
between them during the recycling process. Based on the production data provided by Primewool the 
mass allocation of the outputs was applied as follows: 20% contaminated paper and 80% recycled 
gypsum. 
 
Since the product is still under development and no production process has been implemented, the 
energy consumption had to be assumed in order to build the “Insulation Batt Manufacturing” unit in 
the four models. For the assessed insulation batts two different composing approaches were used 
during the testing (i.e. layers and pulp approach), it was decided that the consumption of electricity 
during the production of the batts would be the same for all the different batts studied (0.05 kWh). 
Nevertheless, for the two batts produced with the pulp approach (i.e. third and fourth ideal insulation 
batt compositions) also some heat (3.65 MJ) will be needed in order to completely dry the insulation 
batts. This amount of heat was calculated from the energy necessary to evaporate the water used to 
make the pulp and it is considered the same for the third and fourth insulation batt compositions. No 
recover or reuse of the water implemented during the manufacturing was considered. The calculations 
related to the heat consumption during the drying process of these two insulation batts compositions 
can be found in Appendix B. No need of heat for the first two insulation batt compositions was 
assumed, since the low amount of water used during the manufacturing process. 
 
Again, since no manufacturing process is implemented for the insulation batts no actual data were 
available, consequently it is assumed that no outputs, by-products or emissions are produced in this 
unit (i.e. Insulation Batts Manufacturing unit) aside from the simple finite product. 
 
In the first two units (i.e. Plasterboard waste Recycling & Refining unit and PRU and Manufacturing 
of the paper-wool unit) a relevant amount of dust is emitted directly to air during the processing. 
During this project it was not possible to quantify these emissions, for this reason it was decided to 
assume 100 g of dust directly emitted to air in both the units. Furthermore, in the second unit (i.e. 
PRU and Manufacturing of the paper-wool unit) filters are installed to keep at an acceptable level the 
dust emissions. The filtered dust is modelled as paperboard for incineration, since the dust is coming 
from the paper-wool production it is assumed that is mainly composed by paper.  
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:80==
Chapter 6. Life Cycle Inventory 
 
64 
6.10 Inventory data sources and quality  
 
The data needed for the modelling phase was collected from both primary and secondary sources. 
Primewool Holding Denmark A/S provided data about the Plasterboard waste Recycling & Refining 
unit and about the PRU and manufacturing of the paper-wool unit, since both processes are already 
implemented and the data is coming from on-site measurements, they can be considered as primary 
data. For what concern the four insulation batts manufacturing units and the disposal stage no primary 
data were available since the manufacturing process is not already implemented and the insulation 
batts are still under development, for these reasons secondary sources were used to gather data such 
as literature research (e.g. kenaf fibres production) and “expert judgment”. A complete list of all the 
processes selected and the elementary flows amounts used in SimaPro with the sources of the data 
implemented during the calculations can be found in Appendix B. 
 
The quality of the data used in this study is assessed applying a pedigree approach proposed by Ciroth 
et al. (2013) based on Weidema & Wesnæs (1996). For each material flow of each unit process the 
quality of the data is assessed with a score with respect to technology, time, geography, completeness 
and reliability. The scale of the score goes from 1 (i.e. “very poor” quality) to 5 (i.e. “very good” 
quality). Table 6.10 below summarizes the applied matrix. In Appendix B it is possible to find the 
data quality profile established for all the material flows of each unit process for all the insulation 
batts compositions studied, applying the below presented matrix.

6.11 Economic life cycle inventory 
 
In the following section the forecasted costs associated with the production of the four different 
insulation batts compositions from the paper-wool production are presented and analysed. As 
previously explained, LCC is not an ISO-standardized tool as LCA, for this reason the guidelines 
dictated by Society of Environmental Toxicology and Chemistry (SETAC, Swarr et al. 2011) were 
followed to perform the life cycle costing from the manufacturer perspective. The aim of this part of 
the study is to assess from an economic point of view the four insulation batt compositions proposed 
after the experiment phase, in order to consider the economic factor together with the environmental 
performances in the further product development. It is important to note that, since the results will be 
used to compare the costs related to the four different compositions the analysis will stop at the finite 
product, i.e. “from cradle to gate”. Furthermore, it is also assumed that the costs related to the usage 
and disposal of the four insulation batt compositions are the same and would not influence this study, 
for this reason they are not considered. 
 
It is important to note that, since the paper-wool used to produce the insulation batts is an already 
commercialized product, the only unit process analysed from an economic point of view is the 
“Insulation Batts Manufacturing” unit for the four different solutions. In fact, data about the 
production costs of paper-wool are already available internally and all the costs related to the 
processes behind its production are completely covered. Furthermore, since the LCC is carried out in 
parallel with the LCA, externalities are not considered to avoid double counting. Since the strict 
boundaries imposed, the limited focus of the study and the LCA conducted in parallel, the LCC can 
be seen as a costs analysis, useful to support decision-making in the further product development and 
to compare the production costs of the four insulation batts with their specific environmental impacts. 
 
The following table (Table 6.11) summarizes the costs related to each material needed for the 
production of the four assessed insulation batts compositions. Note that the costs presented below are 
scaled to the functional unit and are covering only the materials need. A complete overview of all the 
costs related to the production and considered in this study can be found in Appendix B together with 
the calculations to scale the production costs to the previously defined functional unit. 
Table 6.11: Inputs used for the costs analysis of the four compositions. The costs are expressed in euros and scaled to the f.u. [€/f.u.]. 
Input material

0,00664

0,00989 
 
==PAGE:83==
Chapter 7 
 
Life Cycle Impact Assessment 
 
In this section the Life Cycle Impact Assessment (LCIA) is performed to assess the four proposed 
batt composition systems from an environmental point of view. The environmental impact assessment 
method applied in this study is “ReCiPe 2008 (H)”. In accordance with the Goal & Scope definition, 
the LCIA will provide, at midpoint and endpoint level, characterized, internal and external normalized 
and weighted results for all the four analysed different compositions. However, in this section, only 
external normalized results at both midpoint and endpoint level will be presented. All the remaining 
results are presented in Appendix C. It is important to note that in this section all the presented results 
are scaled to the defined functional unit. 
 
7.1 Midpoint 
 
In accordance with the Goal & Scope definition the environmental impacts of the four insulation batt 
compositions are assessed at midpoint using the environmental impact assessment method ReCiPe 
2008 Midpoint (H) and all the impact categories are considered.  
 
7.1.1 External normalization 
 
When characterizing the environmental impacts, the scores of each impact category differed 
considerably in both magnitude and units. For these reasons normalization is necessary to provide a 
better overview and allow an efficient analysis of the environmental impacts. The results were 
normalized both externally and internally, however it was decided to present in this section only the 
externally normalized results since the external references are not dependent on the system under 
study and consequently a more objective comparison can be performed. The internally normalized 
results can be found in Appendix C. First of all, a general overview of the externally normalized 
results will be presented, followed by the analysis of the process contribution for each impact category 
for the four insulation batt compositions under study.  
 
7.1.2 Overview

(Table 7.1) presents the externally normalized results for the four insulation batt compositions. The

Chapter 7. Life Cycle Impact Assessment 
 
69
The external normalization procedure is also useful to check the plausibility of the obtained results. 
For instance, obtaining a result for the impact category Climate change equal to 1 PE means to cause 
an effect equivalent to the impact of an average person in one year.  Of course, this fact is quite 
unlikely for the insulation batts under study, as can be seen in Table 7.1. Only for the impact category 
Water depletion (WD) the normalization factor is equal to zero. The impact assessment method is not 
able to express the damage at endpoint level, and for the characterized results at midpoint level simply 
the total amount of water used is given (ReCiPe, 2008). These features are caused by the lack of a 
global market for water and by the different impacts of its consumption in a dry or wet area (Benini 
et al, 2014; ReCiPe, 2008). For these reasons, it was not possible to externally normalize the obtained 
characterized results for the impact category WD. From the externally normalized results it is possible 
to start the analysis of the four modelled systems. Out of eighteen impact categories (considering also 
WD), the first insulation batt composition is the one with the highest impact scores in eleven 
categories (i.e. OD, ME, HT, PMF, FET, MET, IR, ULO, NLT and both MD and FD). The third 
system is the one with the second number of highest scores in impact categories, four out of eighteen 
(i.e. CC, TA, FE and POF). The second insulation batt composition showed the highest impacts in 
two impact categories (i.e. TET and ALO), whilst the fourth in none. Furthermore, the former showed 
the lowest impacts in thirteen impact categories, while the latter in four. It is important to note that 
even though the first insulation batt composition scored in general the highest environmental impacts, 
in some categories (e.g. ME, HT, ALO) the four insulation batts gave close results (not differing even 
in magnitude). Negative values in Table 7.1 show positive impacts to the environment (i.e. benefits). 
To have an overview of the ratios between the impact scores of the four batts for the same impact 
category it is possible to find the internal normalized results in Appendix C.

Firstly, it is important to clarify that the negative percentages visible in Figure 7.1 represent positive 
impacts, meaning that from the paper-wool manufacturing processes (i.e. Plasterboard waste 
Recycling & Refining unit and the PRU and Manufacturing of the paper-wool unit) for the impact 
categories Climate change, Fossil depletion and Photochemical oxidant formation there are savings 
(blue bars in Figure 7.1). The peculiar behaviour of paper-wool was investigated (see Appendix C); 
and, as expected, the benefits for the environment were mainly due to the high amounts of materials 
recovered during the recycling processes of the C&D waste and the production of paper-wool (e.g. 
gypsum, plastic, iron). In fact, when a raw material is recovered in the systems it is recycled. 
Consequently, it is modelled as it re-enters in its production cycle, avoiding the consumption and 
extraction of other raw materials. For instance, during the paper-wool manufacturing process ferrous 
metals are recovered, this fraction is modelled in SimaPro as an output to Technosphere of iron scraps 
which is going to avoid partially the production and extraction of new ferrous raw materials and 
consequently also the environmental impacts related to these processes. Of course, this trend will be 
observed also in the other three compositions since paper-wool is the main material used in all the 
four compositions and the batts differ only in binders and energy needs. Even though the paper-wool 
production leads to positive impacts in two categories, it is the main contributing process in three 
impact categories (i.e. HT, ALO, ULO). The first insulation batt composition is the one with the 
highest scores in numerous impact categories, thanks to the graph above it is possible to observe that 
the high scores are due mainly to the choice of the glue. The glue (vinyl acetate based) used as binder 
is the main contributor of the environmental impacts in ten out of eighteen impact categories (i.e. CC, 
OD, TA, FE, ME, POF, PMF, IR, NLT, and FD). The disposal process (i.e. incineration) is the main 
contributor in two impact categories (i.e. FET and MET). Both the modelled transport process 
(transport of wood fibres and glue), the water usage, the wood fibres and the electricity showed a 
relatively low influence on the overall impacts. For what concern the water, as explained above, the 
impact category Water depletion cannot be externally normalized. Regarding the electricity, it is 
important to note that the share displayed in the bar chart is related only to the “Insulation Batt 
Manufacturing” unit and do not cover the electricity needs in the other units. However, the impacts 
related to the electricity consumption in the paper-wool production are considered in the graph under 
the paper-wool share. 
 
7.1.4 Composition 2 
 
Figure 7.2 presents the process contribution (in percentages) within each impact category of the 
externally normalized results for the second insulation batt composition assessed, as for the first one 
the modelled building method is dry, i.e. layers approach. Beside the two same positive impacts in 
Climate change and Fossil depletion, in the second composition, the paper-wool production is the 
main contributor in nine impact categories (i.e. OD, TA, FE, HT, PMF, ALO, ULO, NLT and MD). 
Again, the second main contributor is the disposal, in fact, in four impact categories it showed the 
highest impact scores (i.e. ME, POF, FET and MET). It is worth noting that the kenaf fibres are the 
leading contributor in the impact category Terrestrial ecotoxicity, the fact can be justified by the usage 
of various fertilizer during the cultivation of the plants. Also in this composition, the transport 
processes and the water usage do not contribute relevantly to the total impacts. Differently from 
before, the electricity need, in the insulation batt manufacturing process, is the main contributor in 
the impact category Ionising radiation.

As for the first two models previously analysed, the paper-wool production leads to benefits (with 
negative percentages in the graph) in both the impact category Climate change and Fossil depletion. 
This process is also the main contributor of five impact categories (i.e. HT, TET, ALO, ULO and 
MD). Differently from before, the third composition needs heat for the drying process, as can be seen 
in the graph (light-blue) this process contributes relevantly in all the impact categories. In fact, it is 
the main contributor in seven impact categories (i.e. OD, TA, FE, POF, PMF, IR and NLT) and 
consequently, due to the need of heat, the third composition is the one with the highest impacts in 
four impact categories (i.e. CC, TA, FE, POF). The disposal has also a main role in the impacts (dark-
blue in the graph), being the main contributor in two impact categories (i.e. FET, MET). Again, both 
the transport and the water usage do not contribute significantly to the overall scores. However, it is 
important to note that a higher amount of water is used with the pulp approach, and that the Water 
depletion impact category is not displayable. In the characterized results the water is one of the main 
contributors of this impact category (see Appendix C). For what concern the choice of cement, some 
impacts are noted in all the impact categories, however this binder is never the main contributor in 
any of the impact categories considered.

Since the third and the fourth insulation batt compositions are built with the same approach and 
materials except the usage of cement or gypsum as binders, respectively. In the bar chart above it is 
possible to observe the same trends seen in the obtained results for the third composition. Meaning 
high contribution by heat, disposal and paper-wool production for all the impact categories. It is worth 
noticing that in general the choice of gypsum as binder compared to cement showed lower 
environmental impacts in all the eighteen impact categories considered. 
 
7.1.7 Summary 
 
First of all, as expected, being the paper-wool the main material for all the four different compositions, 
it has an important role in the overall impacts. In fact, it has been shown that the paper-wool is one 
of the main contributors in numerous impact categories for the four systems under study. Similar 
trends have been observed in all the four compositions especially for the shared positive impacts 
(negative percentages) in two impact categories (i.e. CC and FD). However, it is important to note 
that the parameter paper-wool, in the graphs, is covering all the units and processes behind the 
production of the material (from the recovery of gypsum to the flame retardant usage). For these 
reasons this main role is justified. It was decided not to present in detail the contributors to the impacts 
of the paper-wool because it is beyond the objectives of this study to analyse in detail the production 
process of the paper-wool. In fact, the analysis is focused on the various compositions and on the 
changes applicable to the implementation of the production of the batts. However, in Appendix C it 
is possible to find an overview of the process contribution for the production of paper-wool. 
 
As for the paper-wool, also the disposal is a process in common for the four compositions under 
study. For the disposal a similar trend has been observed in the four compositions. In fact, this process 
is the main contributor for at least two impact categories (i.e. FET and MET) for all the four batt 
compositions. 
 
For what concern the transport, the externally normalized results showed that this process is not 
influencing considerably the overall impacts of the four systems. The same consideration applies for 
the water, even if the consumption of water with the two approaches are significantly different (layers 
approach vs. pulp approach). However, it is worth noticing that since the impact category Water 
depletion cannot be normalized externally the results for this category are not considered. 
 
All the four systems showed the same trend toward electricity: the process was not contributing 
relevantly to all the impact categories a part from the impact category Ionizing radiation. It is 
important to note that the process electricity in the graphs refers only to the electricity needed in the 
insulation batts manufacturing unit. The electricity usage for the manufacturing of the paper-wool is 
already covered in the paper-wool factor in the graphs. 
 
When comparing the binders in the first two compositions built with the layers approach different 
behaviours were noted. The wood fibres showed marked lower impacts compared to the kenaf fibres, 
whilst the glue (vinyl acetate based) showed much higher impacts than the cellulose ether binder. In 
Appendix C it is possible to find a complete overview of all the environmental impacts of the different 
binders modelled in the four systems.  
==PAGE:90==
Chapter 7. Life Cycle Impact Assessment 
 
74 
7.2 Endpoint 
 
In accordance with the Goal & Scope definition the environmental impacts of the four insulation batt 
compositions are assessed at endpoint level using ReCiPe 2008 Endpoint (H) and all the three damage 
categories (areas of protection) are considered (i.e. Human Health, Ecosystems, and Resources).  
 
7.2.1 External normalization 
 
Again, for the same reasons explained above, the characterized results are presented externally 
normalized. In Appendix C it is possible to find the characterized results and the internally normalized 
results at endpoint level. 
 
7.2.2 Overview 
 
The characterized results were externally normalized by dividing them with the ReCiPe 2008 
normalization factors, the European hierarchist prospective was used. The following table (Table 7.2) 
presents the externally normalized results of the four insulation batt compositions. As for the 
externally normalized impacts at midpoint, at endpoint the results are presented with the unit PE 
(person equivalent). Again, the highest score for each damage category are highlighted in red, while 
the lowest in green. In Appendix C it is possible to find the factors used for the external normalization 
procedure at endpoint. 
 
Table 7.2: Externally normalized results at endpoint level for the four thermal insulation batt composition under study. The highest 
score for each damage category is highlighted in red.

Considering the results displayed in Table 7.2 above, the third insulation batt composition is the one 
with the highest impacts in two (out of three) damage categories (i.e. Human Health and Ecosystem). 
The first composition is the one with the highest score in the damage category Resources. As at 
midpoint level, the first and the third insulation batt compositions are the ones with the highest scores. 
It is worth noticing that for the damage category Resources, the first composition gives a result 
considerably higher than the other three (more than one order of magnitude). A peculiar case are the 
results for the second insulation batt composition, they are all negative (i.e. positive impacts) and the 
lowest for each damage category. For this composition, the savings due to the recycling of the C&D 
waste and production of paper-wool are much higher than the impacts due to the production of the 
insulation batt in all the three damage categories. This feature was not observed in all the impact 
categories at midpoint level (there were categories with positive impact scores), consequently when 
the impact scores were converted from midpoint to endpoint and grouped, the savings outweighed 
the environmental impacts. The following bar chart (Figure 7.5) presents graphically the externally 
normalized results listed above.

compositions will be presented in detail and analysed. It is important to note that the results at 
endpoint level are characterized by a higher uncertainty compared to the midpoint ones due to more 
modelling assumptions. On the other hand results at endpoint level are easier to communicate and 
interpret also for non-technical audience compared to the midpoint ones.
