7.2.7 Summary 
 
First of all, the analysis of the contribution of paper-wool showed positive impacts (negative 
percentages) in the three damage categories for all the four compositions. In some cases, the savings 
were so high that the overall score of a damage category resulted negative. This fact was observed in 
all the three damage categories for the second insulation batt composition and for the damage category 
Resources for the fourth composition. 
 
As at midpoint level, also at endpoint it was confirmed that the transport, the water usage and the 
electricity do not have a relevant influence on the overall impacts. Of course, what have been said 
previously about water (Water depletion normalization factor missing) and electricity (covering only 
the manufacturing) is still valid also at endpoint level. 
 
Regarding the first insulation batt composition, the results obtained at midpoint were confirmed also 
at endpoint, meaning that the glue (vinyl acetate based) is the main contributor to the overall 
environmental impacts. Moreover, when compared directly to the other binders the vinyl acetated 
based glue is the one with the highest environmental scores (see the overview of the characterized 
results in Appendix C). Considering the fibres implemented, also at endpoint level, the wood fibres 
performed better from an environmental point of view than the kenaf fibres, contributing modestly to 
the overall impacts of the first model. Finally, as at midpoint level, the drying process for the third 
and fourth compositions is the main contributor to the overall impacts.

7.3 Costs analysis 
 
In accordance with the Goal & Scope definition of this study the four different compositions are 
assessed also from an economic point of view. Table 7.3 below summarizes the total costs related to 
the production of the four insulation batt compositions under study. The costs are presented in euros 
and scaled to the functional unit. 
 
Table 7.3: Forecasted total costs related to the production of the four insulation batt compositions under study. The costs are presented 
in euros and scaled to the functional unit [€/f.u.]. 
Ideal composition 
Total costs [€/f.u.] 
Batt 1 
0,6739 
Batt 2 
0,5158 
Batt 3 
0,5054 
Batt 4 
0,4907 
 
 
As can be seen in Table 7.3 above, the first ideal composition is the one with the forecasted highest 
costs, followed by the second, the third and the fourth. It is important to note that the costs related to 
the machinery for the production of the batts or the wages of the workers were not considered in the 
calculations for one main reason: these costs are assumed to be almost equals for the four different 
compositions. Also externalities were not considered to avoid double counting with the LCIA results. 
A complete and detailed overview of the calculations and of all the costs considered can be found in 
Appendix C. 
 
The following bar-chart (Figure 7.10) presents in detail the factors contributing to the forecasted costs 
of the production of each insulation batt composition. As before, the costs are presented in euros and 
scaled to the previously defined functional unit (€/f.u.). Since the transport costs are not influencing 
considerably the total costs, it was decided to present them grouped. It is important to note that the 
transport is related only to the binders (i.e. wood fibres, vinyl acetate, kenaf fibres, cellulose ether, 
cement, and gypsum) and that the same assumptions regarding the transport made during LCIA were 
applied also through the costs analysis. The transport of paper-wool is not considered since the batts 
manufacturing process is meant to be implemented in series. In Appendix C it is also possible to find 
a complete overview of all the calculations and costs related to the transport. Moreover, as explained 
in the economic inventory section (Chapter 6), the paper-wool factor costs are covering all the costs 
related to the production of the material and presented grouped since it is beyond the objectives of 
this project to analyses in detail also this production process. 
 
==PAGE:96==
Chapter 7. Life Cycle Impact Assessment 
 
80 
 
Figure 10: Costs analysis of each insulation batt composition under study. The costs are presented in euros and scaled to the functional 
unit. Also the contribution of each parameter to the total costs is presented. The Transport parameter in the graph refers to the transport 
costs of the binders used to build the batts (i.e. wood fibres, Vinyl acetate, kenaf fibres, cellulose ether, cement, gypsum). 
 
In Figure 7.10 it is possible to note that the paper-wool is the highest contributing factor to the total 
costs for all the four compositions (blue in the graph). This can be considered as a plausible result, in 
fact, as explained above, the paper-wool cost covers all the processes and the materials needed for 
the finite product (from the recycling of the C&D waste to the manufacturing of the paper-wool). The 
bar chart shows also that the electricity cost and the water usage do not influence considerably the 
total costs of production. The main contributors, apart from the paper-wool, are the glue (vinyl 
acetate) for the first composition, the kenaf fibre for the second one and the heat for the last two 
compositions. It is interesting to note that a similar trend was observed for the major contributors of 
the previously analysed environmental impacts. In the discussion section (Chapter 8) the correlation 
between the environmental impacts and the economic costs will be analysed in detail.

Chapter 8 
 
Interpretation & Discussion 
 
In this chapter, the results of the LCI and LCIA will be discussed and interpreted further. Starting 
with the identification of the significant issues, then a completeness, consistency and 
representativeness checks of the LCA are carried out. Two sensitivity analysis of the four models are 
performed (i.e. perturbation analysis and scenario analysis). Finally, the results of the LCIA and the 
production costs analysis will be coupled and analysed simultaneously in order to understand in deep 
these two aspects. 
 
8.1 Identification of significant issues 
 
In the following section the results obtained during the LCIA will be structured and analysed in order 
to identify the significant issues. According to the ILCD handbook (European Commission, 2010), it 
is possible to divide the aspects of significant issues in two categories: the main contributors to the 
environmental impacts of the system and the main choices made during the building of the model 
with the potential to significantly influence the overall impacts. 
 
8.1.1 Main contributors 
 
During the presentation of the externally normalized results at both midpoint and endpoint level, the 
main contributors to the environmental impacts of the four systems under study were identified. The 
first relevant contributor is the paper-wool, used in all the four compositions, with both negative and 
positive impacts. The disposal is also a common and relevant contributor, in fact the LCIA underlined 
the key impacts due to this phase. Analysing the results of the first insulation batt composition it was 
observed that the choice of the glue (vinyl acetate based) has a relevant role on the overall 
environmental impacts. Regarding the second system, a part from the already mentioned paper-wool 
and disposal method, also the kenaf fibres production showed a significant share in the total impacts. 
Finally, the environmental impacts of the third and fourth compositions were characterized by the 
main role of the heat (drying process). In this chapter, all the parameters listed above will be tested 
and investigated through a sensitivity analysis to understand in deep their role in the systems. 
 
8.1.2 Main Choices 
 
In order to build the insulation batts manufacturing unit, since the finite product is still under 
development, it was necessary to make assumptions and consequently simplify considerably the 
hypothetical manufacturing process. From the results obtained, it was observed that this unit process 
contributes with a significant share to the overall impacts for the four systems. Consequently, choices 
==PAGE:98==
Chapter 8. Interpretation & Discussion 
82 
made such as no outputs to the Technosphere during the batt manufacturing process or assumption 
of the need of energy, could have a high influence on the final results. 
 
Regarding the third and fourth batt compositions, it was decided to consider a certain amount of heat 
to simulate the drying process during the manufacturing of the insulation batts. The amount needed 
was calculated from the energy necessary to evaporate the water used in the pulp process (minus 
some dripping, see Appendix B). From the impact scores obtained, it is clear that this choice is 
influencing considerably the results of these two systems.  
 
During the building of the models, it was assumed incineration as disposal method. Throughout the 
main contributors analysis, it was observed that the disposal phase has a significant influence on the 
final results. Consequently, the choice of this method could have a relevant weight on the obtained 
environmental scores. 
 
Finally, the choice of considering as burden free the C&D waste to produce the paper-wool is giving 
to the overall impacts the peculiar positive scores (savings) in two impact categories. Clearly this 
choice is influencing significantly the results. However, when the four systems are compared, this 
choice is not significant since all of them are made with paper-wool. 
 
8.2 Completeness check 
 
In the following section the completeness of the four built models is checked and analysed. In 
accordance with the Goal & Scope definition, it was decided to check whether the setup of the four 
models and the environmental impact assessment can be considered as complete.  
 
8.2.1 Life cycle Inventory 
 
The first two unit processes (i.e. “Plasterboard waste Recycling & Refining” and “PRU and 
Manufacturing of paper-wool”) can be considered entirely covered, since all the inputs and outputs 
involved were considered and primary data were gathered. Only few modelling assumptions and 
simplifications were necessary, but it is believed that they did not influence the obtained results (e.g. 
assumed emissions of dust to air). Furthermore, the positive impacts (benefits) obtained during the 
modelling of the paper-wool production due to the recovering of raw materials were equals in all the 
four systems and confirmed the correct building of the models. 
 
Regarding the insulation batts manufacturing unit, as explained above, simplifications and 
assumptions were necessary to model a hypothetical process. For instance, it was assumed that the 
manufacturing unit has no outputs and no emissions apart from the finite insulation batt and that the 
electricity consumption was equal for the two different building approaches (i.e. layers and pulp 
approach). For these reasons, this unit cannot be considered as complete as the first two. 
 
For what concern the disposal, also for this phase, some assumptions were necessary (e.g. model the 
batts as waste paperboard to municipal waste, and disregarding of the transport), however since the 
process was implemented from an already existing process in SimaPro this unit can be considered 
==PAGE:99==
Chapter 8. Interpretation & Discussion 
 
83
complete. It is believed that this assumption would not influence considerably the results since paper-
wool is the main materials of the insulation batts. 
 
Finally, regarding the transport, when not already covered by the databases in SimaPro, it was decided 
to consider this factor only for the main materials needed for the building of the insulations batts. 
Consequently, the transport for few processes, not directly related with the manufacturing of the batts 
(e.g. fungicide during the production of paper-wool), is not covered. However, it is believed that the 
results obtained would not be influenced by the lack of only these few transport processes, since the 
transport factors included did not influence considerably the results. 
 
8.2.2 Environmental Impact assessment 
 
In accordance with the Goal & Scope definition, during the LCIA phase the environmental impacts 
of the four systems were characterized, internally normalized, externally normalized and weighted at 
both midpoint and endpoint level. Being all the listed steps fulfilled, the impact assessment can be 
considered as fully completed. It is worth noticing that, only for the impact category Water depletion, 
the results could not be externally normalized for the lack of a normalization factor compatible with 
the environmental impact assessment method. 
 
8.3 Representativeness check 
 
In this section the representativeness of the model built in SimaPro is checked from a geographical, 
technological and time-related point of view. The aim is to assess each unit process implemented in 
the program and to understand whether the model is representative for the system under study. As for 
the data quality, the representativeness of the data is assessed using a scale from 1 to 5 (1 denotes 
“very low representativeness” and 5 “very high representativeness”). In Appendix D it is possible to 
find a table summarizing the representativeness of each process implemented in the entire model.  
 
8.3.1 Geographical representativeness 
 
In accordance with the Goal & Scope of this study the model is representative for Denmark. 
Consequently, processes from Denmark had a higher priority when implemented in the model since 
their high representativeness. When no Danish processes were available in the SimaPro databases, 
average data for Europe were used instead. Those data were considered of medium geographical 
representativeness. In case not even data from Europe were available, global processes were 
implemented (low representativeness). 
 
8.3.2 Technological representativeness 
 
According to the Goal & Scope definition, the technological representativeness of the models is the 
present technology level for the production of the paper-wool and also for the future implementations 
to produce the insulation batts. During the building of the model, processes having the same 
technological level were assumed to be representative for the applied technologies in the four systems 
==PAGE:100==
Chapter 8. Interpretation & Discussion 
84 
under study (high representativeness). When the technological level of the implemented process was 
lower or not defined at all, the representativeness was considered as low. 
 
8.3.3 Time-related representativeness 
 
In accordance with the Goal & Scope definition, in general, the present time is considered for the 
temporal representativeness. However, the plasterboard recycling unit was installed in 2008 and the 
paper-wool manufacturing unit in 2015. For those two units, the time representativeness is considered 
2008 for the former and 2015 for the latter. When the age of the processes implemented in the models 
were in line with the years of the units, the time-related representativeness was considered as high. 
 
8.4 Consistency check 
 
In the following section the consistency between the Goal & Scope definition, the four models and 
the results obtained will be briefly checked. First of all, it is confirmed that the LCI modelling 
framework applied is the attributional. As defined in the Goal & Scope definition, in the first unit (i.e. 
Plasterboard waste Recycling & Refining unit), allocation was performed between the recovery of 
gypsum and the contaminated paper, and the input of C&D waste was modelled as burden free. Since 
this study has a comparative nature, also the definition of the functional unit, system boundaries and 
assumptions were made with consistency for the four systems under study and in accordance with the 
Goal & Scope definition. For what concern the method chosen to evaluate the environmental impacts, 
the guidelines dictated in the first phase of the LCA study were followed completely: same EIAM 
applied for the four systems at midpoint and endpoint, same normalizations factors implemented and 
weighting was performed. Finally, it is confirmed that, the paper-wool, used as main material in all 
the four insulation batt compositions, and the disposal method are modelled in the same way for the 
four systems under study. This feature is fundamental since the comparative nature of the study. 
 
8.5 Sensitivity analysis 
 
In this section the robustness of the four models is tested throughout a sensitivity analysis. During a 
sensitivity analysis, it is investigated how the outputs (environmental impacts in this case) of a model 
change with the varying of the inputs. Two different studies were performed to analyse the behaviour 
of the models in changes of: the continuous parameters (i.e. perturbation analysis) and of the 
discontinuous parameters (i.e. scenario analysis). Not all the parameters present in the four models 
were tested, but only the ones believed to be the more relevant and important for the results of this 
study. 
 
8.5.1 Perturbation analysis 
 
The sensitivity of the model was tested on the main contributors, on the main materials and on the 
main assumptions. The sensitivity analysis on the continuous parameters was performed increasing 
by 10% (one by one) the studied values. With the change in the outputs it is possible to calculate the 
Sensitivity Index (SI), considering outputs and inputs of the model before and after the perturbation. 
==PAGE:101==
Chapter 8. Interpretation & Discussion 
 
85
The following equation was implemented to calculate each SI during the perturbation analysis 
(Rosenbaum & Fantke, 2016; Laurent, 2016):  
 
𝑆𝐼= 
( ∆𝑜𝑢𝑡𝑝𝑢𝑡
𝑜𝑢𝑡𝑝𝑢𝑡 )
( ∆𝑖𝑛𝑝𝑢𝑡
𝑖𝑛𝑝𝑢𝑡 )
 
 
 
Where ∆output represents the change in the environmental score (result) and ∆input is the change in 
the parameter tested. Since during this study all the parameters are increased by 10%, the dominator 
of the SI will always be equal to 0,10. During the perturbation analysis, the threshold of sensitivity is 
posed at 1, consequently when a SI higher or equal to 1 is obtained the model is considered sensitive 
to the tested parameter. It is worth noting that a SI equal to 1 means that a rise of 10% of the input 
increase the total results by 10%. In Table 8.1 below the continuous parameters tested for each 
composition under study are summarized. Since this study focuses on the different compositions it 
was decided to not test the parameters related to the production of paper-wool, but to focus instead 
on the parameters (materials and energy) implement during the manufacturing of the insulation batts. 
 
Table 8.1: Continuous parameters tested during the sensitivity analysis for each insulation batt composition under study. During the 
perturbation analysis the parameters were increased one by one by 10%.

common for all the four compositions, consequently they will be tested four times, one for each 
system. The results obtained through the sensitivity analysis are summarized in Table 8.2 below. The 
SI were calculated increasing one by one the tested parameters by 10% and applying to the results 
obtained the formula presented above. In Table 8.2 the SI highlighted in red show the parameters for 
which the model is considered sensitive (the threshold was defined as ≥ 1). Also, negative values of 
SI were obtained, in these cases the sensitivity threshold was set as ≤ -1. As during the LCIA, all the 
impact categories were considered during the sensitivity analysis.

Chapter 8. Interpretation & Discussion

Considering the defined threshold for sensitivity, it can be noted that the four models are sensitive 
only to three main parameters: paper-wool, glue and heat. In fact, all the systems are sensitive to the 
paper-wool input, the model of the first composition is sensitive also to the glue (vinyl acetate based), 
whilst the third and the fourth combinations to the heat (drying process). Furthermore, it is worth 
noting that only two impact categories showed high SI, i.e. Climate change and Fossil depletion. 
Furthermore, a negative SI value in Table 8.2 shows simply a decreasing in the environmental impacts 
(more benefits for the environment). The obtained negative values are related to the parameter paper-
wool, which for the impact categories CC, POF and FD, showed negative environmental impacts 
(benefits for the environment) during the LCIA phase. Consequently, when increasing by 10% the 
input of paper-wool in the models more benefits for the environment are expected in those impact 
categories. The peculiar behaviour on the environmental impacts of paper-wool for the four models 
was investigated; and, as expected, the benefits for the environment were mainly due to the high 
amounts of materials recovered during the recycling processes of the C&D waste (e.g. gypsum, 
plastic, iron). 
 
Regarding the first composition, the tests showed that the model is also sensitive to the glue (vinyl 
acetate) parameter. For instance, in the impact category Climate change, a SI equal to 3,43 was 
obtained, meaning that when increasing the input of glue by 10%, the impact score in this category 
increased by 34,3%. Even though the model was sensitive to this parameter in only two impact 
categories, it is worth underlying that high SI were obtained also in the other impacts categories (SI 
≥ 0,5 in nine categories out of eighteen). This binder was also one of the main contributors to the 
environmental impacts of this composition (see LCIA, Chapter 7). The first model was observed not 
sensitive to the changes of the other parameters (i.e. disposal, electricity and wood fibres). It is 
interesting to emphasise that SI equal to 0 were obtained when increasing the parameter wood fibres. 
This fact means that even though the input parameter was increased by 10%, the environmental scores 
in certain impact categories did not variate at all (eleven impact categories out of 18). Consequently, 
it can be concluded that the production of this material does not result in impacts in those categories 
at all. 
 
Paper-wool is the only parameter for which the model of the second composition is sensitive (two 
impact categories, i.e. CC, FD). For this composition, the kenaf fibres were one of the main 
contributors to the environmental impacts, however the perturbation analysis showed that the model 
is not sensitive to this parameter. Moreover, the model is not sensitive also to the other parameters 
tested: the disposal, the electricity and the cellulose ether. 
 
Regarding the third and fourth compositions, a part from the common parameter paper-wool, these 
two models were observed sensitive to the changes of the parameter heat. The heat, needed for the 
drying process of the insulation batts, was identified as one of the main contributors to the 
environmental impacts of the last two ideal compositions. Furthermore, the highest SI were observed 
in the impact category CC for the fourth composition for both paper-wool and heat. An explanation 
of this fact is that the environmental impacts due to the other parameters (e.g. gypsum and electricity) 
are so low and irrelevant (in comparison) that when increasing these two parameters (i.e. paper-wool 
and heat) the environmental impacts are drastically increased. 
 
 
==PAGE:104==
Chapter 8. Interpretation & Discussion 
88 
8.5.2 Scenario analysis 
 
Differently from the continuous parameters, with the discontinuous ones it is not possible to calculate 
the SI of the model since the changes in the parameters is not quantifiable. In fact, in this section, the 
parameters will not be increased by 10% but changed with a different process to evaluate the 
sensitivity of the model to the choices made during LCI phase (building of the model). Again, only 
the main assumptions, the main contributors and the main materials to compose the insulation batts 
will be tested. The following formula was implemented during the scenario analysis to calculate the 
relative change using the impact scores (IS) (Rosenbaum & Fantke, 2016; Laurent, 2016): 
 
 
%𝑅𝑒𝑙𝑎𝑡𝑖𝑣𝑒 𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑛𝑔𝑒= ( 𝐼𝑆 𝑠𝑐𝑒𝑛𝑎𝑟𝑖𝑜−𝐼𝑆 𝑏𝑎𝑠𝑒 𝑐𝑎𝑠𝑒)
𝐼𝑆 𝑏𝑎𝑠𝑒 𝑐𝑎𝑠𝑒
 
 
 
Where IS base case represents the impact score before the change, whilst IS scenario is the impact 
score obtained with the tested scenario. It was decided to define the model as sensitive to a change 
when the relative change was equal or higher of 100%, meaning at least a factor of 2. Table 8.3 below 
summarizes the processes tested during the sensitivity analysis of the discontinuous parameters 
(scenario analysis). In Appendix D it is possible to find a complete and detailed overview of all the 
processes changed during the sensitivity analysis. 
  
Table 8.3: Discontinuous parameters tested during the sensitivity analysis for each insulation batt composition.  
Parameter 
Composition: 
1 
 
2 
 
3 
 
4

X 
 
Starting from the disposal, it was decided to test this process for three main reasons: it was based on 
an assumption (i.e. incineration), the batts were modelled as cardboard waste to municipal 
incineration and it showed a relevant contribution to the overall environmental impacts in all the four 
systems under study. During the scenario analysis, the disposal method was changed from 
incineration to landfill in all the four systems. Even though this solution is unlikely for Denmark it 
can be still a possibility for other European countries. The following bar chart (Figure 8.1) presents 
graphically the results obtained. In Appendix D it is possible to find the above displayed results 
summarized in a table and the processes changed in detail. 
 
==PAGE:105==
Chapter 8. Interpretation & Discussion 
 
89
 
Figure 8.1: Relative changes [%] obtained for the scenario analysis of the disposal method. The tests were run for all the four different 
insulation compositions. The disposal method was changed from incineration to landfill. All the eighteen impact categories are 
displayed. 
 
Considering the decided threshold for sensitivity (i.e. relative changes higher than 100%), Figure 8.1 
above shows that none of the four systems is sensitive to the disposal method chosen for all the 
eighteen impact categories considered. As for the perturbation analysis, negative relative changes 
represent lower environmental scores due to the process change.  As it can be seen in Figure 8.1, for 
all the four systems, positive percentages were obtained only in two impact categories (i.e. ULO and 
ALO). The higher scores in these two impact categories are driven by the fact that a landfill is 
characterized by a higher usage of land than an incineration. It is important to note that the 
incineration disposal method was changed to an inert landfill and that all the benefits obtained with 
the change can be justified by the fact that the applied process for landfill does not include the 
emissions after disposal. Consequently, lower impacts in comparison with the incineration method 
are observed. For instance, for the impact category Climate change, driven by emission of carbon 
dioxide (CO2) and methane (CH4), lower scores are displayed since the landfill acts as a carbon sink.  
 
Also the electricity process was investigated for all the four systems. It was decided to change the 
national Danish grid mix process applied to a wind energy production. The choice is justified by the 
high application of this energy production technology in the country of the systems under study (i.e. 
Denmark). The following bar chart (Figure 8.2) presents the calculated relative changes of the impact 
scores due to the new applied process. In Appendix D it is possible to find the results displayed below 
summarized in a table and the processes changed in detail. 
-80
-70

Figure 8.2: Relative changes [%] obtained for the scenario analysis of the electricity input. The tests were run for all the four different 
insulation compositions. The electricity input was changed from national grid mix to windmill production. All the eighteen impact 
categories are displayed. 
 
As for the disposal method tested, none of the system appeared sensitive to the change in the 
electricity input. Again, mainly negative relative changes are observed; a part from the impact 
category Metal depletion where the positive percentages calculated can be defined irrelevant, in fact 
almost same impact scores were obtained in this impact category after the change (relative change at 
least lower than 0,20% in the four systems). The higher relative changes are observed in the impact 
categories where the input electricity was one of the main contributors to the total impact scores (i.e. 
IR and ALO, see LCIA section). It is important to note that the electricity input tested is covering 
only the energy needs of the insulation batts production unit and do not include the entire consumption 
in the systems (i.e. manufacturing of paper-wool and C&D waste recycling electricity needs not 
included). As during the perturbation analysis, the other electricity inputs were not studied in detail 
since belonging to the paper-wool production process. 
 
One of the main contributor of the third and fourth compositions (pulp approach) was the need of 
energy for the drying process. The input of heat was tested also in the scenario analysis. It was decided 
to change the process from “central or small scale” to “district or industrial” (see Appendix D for the 
complete processes). The following bar chart (Figure 8.3) graphically displays the relative changes 
calculated due to the application of the tested processes.

Figure 8.3: Relative changes [%] obtained for the scenario analysis of the heat input (drying process). The tests were run for the third 
(Batt 3) and fourth (Batt 4) insulation compositions. The heat input was changed from “central or small scale” to “district or 
industrial”. All the eighteen impact categories are displayed. 
 
The fourth composition model resulted sensible to the change in the heat input only for the impact 
category Climate change (relative change higher than 100%). While, the model of the third 
composition was not sensitive to the changed input in any impact category. The highest relative 
changes were observed in both models for the impact categories CC, ALO and PMF respectively. 
The results obtained confirmed the role of the heat input in the overall impacts of the third and fourth 
compositions. In fact, even changing process similar results were obtained. In Appendix D it is 
possible to find the displayed results summarized in a table and the processes changed in detail. 
 
During the scenario analysis, also the specific binders of the different compositions were tested. For 
the first system both the wood fibres and the glue (vinyl acetate) were investigated, in both studies 
the system resulted not sensitive to the changes in the processes. The glue was tested twice since its 
important role in the overall impacts (main contributor), however relative changes always lower than 
20% were observed. The wood fibres input was changed to a “sustainable forest management” 
process (in line with the actual fibres tested), similar results were obtained in seventeen impact 
categories out of eighteen (relative changes lower than 4%). The only exception was observed in the 
impact category Agricultural land occupation with a relative change equivalent to 583%. Clearly in 
the first process used the land occupation is not considered since the plants (for the wood) are 
supposed to grow wildly and not in a “human controlled way” as for the “sustainable management” 
process. Regarding the second system, the cellulose ether binder input was tested twice. The model 
resulted not sensitive to both changes (relative changes lower than 4% in all the impact categories).

In the second model, also the kenaf fibres input was tested since this process was built from scratch 
following the case study of Ardente et al. (2008) and it was used instead of a kenaf fibres process 
present in the databases in SimaPro. Even though high relative changes were obtained in some impact 
categories, the established threshold of sensitivity was not passed in any of the eighteen impact 
categories. Consequently, the study showed that the second system is not sensitive to the change 
tested. Regarding the third and fourth compositions under study, both the cement and the gypsum 
were tested during the analysis. Again, the models did not result sensitive to the changes.  
 
To sum up, during the scenario analysis, considering the sensitivity threshold established, the four 
models were not found sensitive to almost all the changes tested, besides some exceptions specific to 
certain impact categories. All the results obtained during the scenario analysis are summarized and 
presented in detail in Appendix D.

8.6 Environmental impacts vs. production costs 
 
In this section the results obtained from the LCIA and the costs analysis will be compared and 
investigated together. Similar trends were observed during both analyses. In fact, it was noted that 
for all the four compositions the paper-wool input was one of the main contributors from an economic 
and environmental point of view. Regarding the first composition, the applied glue (vinyl acetate 
based) was one of the main actors from both perspectives. Furthermore, from the analyses of the third 
and fourth compositions the main contribution of the drying process to the total costs and to the 
overall environmental impacts was observed. 
 
The following graph (Figure 8.4) presents, for each insulation batt composition, a comparison 
between the total costs of production and the environmental impacts for the midpoint impact category 
Climate change (CC). All the presented data are scaled to the defined functional unit. It was decided 
to present and analyse in detail the impact category CC since it is one of the most actual, recognised 
and discussed impact categories. 
 
 
Figure 8.4: Production costs [euro] compared to the environmental impacts of the midpoint impact category Climate change (CC) [kg 
CO2 eq.]. The results are scaled to the functional unit. 
 
Firstly, from Figure 8.4 it is possible to note that, unfortunately, there is not a cheapest and most 
ecofriendly solution. In fact, composition 4 is the one showing the lowest production costs, while 
composition 2 is the one with the lowest environmental impacts. Nevertheless, some interesting 
results can be observed. For instance, even though composition 2 and composition 3 have similar 
production costs, they are the two compositions with the highest and the lowest impact scores for the 
impact category CC. Furthermore, composition 2, 3 and 4 showed similar production costs but 
different impact scores, if the little differences between their production costs were considered 
irrelevant, composition 2 would be the best solution. However, it is important to note that Figure 8.4 
-0,4
-0,3
-0,2

==PAGE:110==
Chapter 8. Interpretation & Discussion 
94 
is representing only for the impact category CC. In Appendix D it is possible to find graphs presenting 
the correlation between production costs and impact scores for all the remaining seventeen impact 
categories.  
 
When considering the costs and the impact scores for the four compositions in all the eighteen impact 
categories, common trends and patterns can be observed. Firstly, since the production costs are fixed 
for each impact category considered, the first composition is always the one with the highest 
production costs in all the impact categories. Furthermore, it is also the one with the highest impact 
scores in twelve impact categories (i.e. OD, ME, HT, PMF, FET, MET, IR, ULO, NLT, WD and both 
MD and FD). As explained above, those results are mainly drive, apart from the paper-wool, by the 
choice of glue used. It is worth noting that the fourth composition is the only batt showing at the same 
time best scores from both an environmental and economic point of view, in fact in four impact 
categories it appears as the cheapest and more ecofriendly solution (i.e. ME, TET, ALO and MD).  
 
Taking into account the second, the third and the fourth batt compositions, due to some uncertainty 
related to the forecasted production costs, it is justified to consider the differences in their three 
production costs irrelevant. Since the price factor is considered very similar, the same conclusions 
drawn during the LCIA are still valid and can be applied. Meaning, the second composition compared 
to the other two showed the lowest impacts in fourteen impact categories (i.e. CC, OD, TA, FE, HT, 
POF, PMF, FET, MET, IR ULO, NLT, WD and FD), while the fourth in four (i.e. ME, TET, ALO, 
MD). Even though, in general, the second composition performed better from both an environmental 
and economic point of view, in some impact categories (e.g. ME, HT, ALO) the four insulation batts 
gave close results. From the analysis it is noted that the main advantage of the second composition 
when compared to the third and fourth is the different building approach (i.e. layers approach). In 
fact, avoiding the heat consumption for the drying process (needed with the pulp approach) saves to 
this system a considerable amount of environmental impacts and production costs. 
 
In conclusion, when the results of the LCIA and of the costs analysis are brought together, and a 
trade-off between the impact scores in all the eighteen impact categories and the production costs is 
considered, it is the second ideal composition the one with the best performances from an economic 
and environmental point of view. It is important to note that the role of the building approaches (layers 
approach vs. pulp approach) influenced considerably the results, in fact it is the energy needed for 
the drying process the tiebreaker of the comparison. 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:111==
 
Chapter 9 
 
Conclusion & Recommendations 
 
This study covered the early phase of the product development process, investigating the 
environmental and economic profiles of the prototypes obtained with the first tests run. All the 
experiments conducted were presented and discussed in detail, together with the behaviour of the 
paper-wool and the other binders applied. From the results of the experiments four insulation batt 
compositions were chosen and assessed from both an environmental and an economic point of view. 
The obtained results are intended to support and guide further the product development process and 
to give an insight and a wider overview of the potential environmental impacts generated by the 
choices made during the product development phase. 
 
The four insulation batt compositions assessed were characterized by two different building methods, 
i.e. layers approach and pulp approach. These two different building methods, applied during the 
experiments, were intended to simulate the two most widely used manufacturing technologies for the 
production of cellulose-based boards and insulating batts, i.e. dry process and wet process 
(InTechFibres, 2017). When comparing the methods, the LCIA study and the costs analysis showed 
that the main drawback of the wet method is the high need of energy. In fact, the heat (drying process) 
was one of the main contributors to both the total costs and the overall environmental impacts of the 
third and fourth insulation batt compositions. This suggests that the R&D department of Primewool 
Holding Denmark should focus the product development on a dry process solution, instead of a wet 
one.  
 
The layers approach (dry building process) showed the best trade-off between overall environmental 
impacts and total production costs. However, it is worth noting that also the choices of the binders 
influenced substantially the environmental and economic performances. In fact, even though the first 
composition was modelled with the layers approach, due to the binder chosen (vinyl acetate based) 
higher impacts than the third and fourth compositions (pulp approach) were observed. Therefore, 
even if in general the dry building method is the best in performance, the application of the “wrong” 
binder can irremediably undermine both the environmental and economic profile of the insulation 
batts. 
 
Since this study did not focus only on the batts manufacturing per se but also on the different materials 
implemented during the tests, others conclusions can be drawn from both the LCA and the costs 
analysis. The wood fibres (as binder) acted better that the kenaf ones from both perspectives. In fact, 
even though the second composition (with kenaf fibres) performed better than the first one (with 
wood fibres), when analysing the process contribution, the usage of the kenaf fibres led to higher 
impact scores than wood fibres in all the eighteen impact categories considered. Also the costs related 
to the usage of wood fibres are lower than the kenaf fibres ones. These outcomes suggest that further 
product development studies should focus on the wood fibres (and on possible alternatives) rather 
than kenaf fibres. Regarding the two glues modelled in this study, i.e. vinyl acetate based and 
==PAGE:112==
Chapter 9. Conclusion & Recommendations 
 
 
96 
cellulose ether, the latter gave better results from both an economic and an environmental perspective. 
In fact, the LCIA highlighted that the vinyl acetate based binder was one of the main contributors of 
the first composition. It is suggested that the product development process should focus further on 
the cellulose ether as binder (and on possible alternatives), rather than on vinyl acetate based glues.  
 
In this study an insulation batt (layers approach) made of paper-wool, wood fibres and cellulose ether 
was not assessed. However, from the contribution analysis it is possible to conclude that this 
composition would be the best solution from both points of view. In fact, those binders and building 
method were the ones performing better throughout the study. This ideal presented composition 
should not be seen as a final and ultimate solution, but rather as an outcome of the first experiments 
and of the first modelling phase (early phase of the product development). It is from there that the 
product development should proceed further considering new binders and implementing the already 
existing LCA models.  
 
Regarding the binders, it has been already explained in the experiments chapter that the choices of 
the materials implemented during the experiments, and consequently considered through the entire 
study, were influenced by the timeframe of the project, the budget constraints and by the availability 
of specific technical instruments to perform the tests. The further product development should 
consider also new and diverse binders for two main reasons. Firstly, it has been shown, with the vinyl 
acetate glue case, that the choice of the binder could have a large influence on the overall results. 
Secondly, during the experiments, binders widely applied in the sector such as natural resins, pMDI 
(polymeric 4,4'-Methylene diphenyl isocyanate) or textile fibres (BICO fibres) were not tested for the 
reasons explained above (i.e. timeframe, budget and technical instruments constrains and limitations) 
(InTechFibres, 2017).  
 
For what concerns the LCA models, the systems built throughout this study have the potential to be 
easily modified and implemented again in the further product development process. In fact, in case 
of future new solutions (e.g. binders) or more certain and quantified parameters (e.g. energy 
consumption in the batts manufacturing unit) the modified models can be applied again in order to 
assess the new compositions. The first two units of the models would be always the same (i.e. 
recycling of C&D waste and production of paper-wool), and the adjustments would be needed only 
in the batts manufacturing unit, as the paper-wool is the main material for all the possible solutions 
and it is produced through the first two units. Furthermore, the first two units were assessed gathering 
primary data and their completeness was checked. The batt manufacturing unit is the only one with a 
lack of primary data since still modelled as hypothetical (not already implemented). In the future, 
new and more complete data about the manufacturing unit could be gathered in order to improve the 
representativeness of the batt manufacturing unit and consequently of the entire system.  From the 
analysis of the models, it was observed that even though the paper-wool parameter is one of the main 
contributors, the higher uncertainty and lack of completeness is found in the insulation batts 
manufacturing unit and not in the paper-wool manufacturing process.  
 
From the results of the LCIA the most significant learning to highlight is the importance of the 
application of the concepts of recycling and upcycling. In fact, the environmental impacts of the four 
systems studied were all driven and influenced by the benefits to the environment due to the recycling 
of the C&D waste. The benefits were mainly due to the high amount of raw materials recovered 
==PAGE:113==
Chapter 9. Conclusions & Recommendations 
 
 
97
during the recycling and upcycling processes (e.g. gypsum, metals, plastics). The LCIA showed that 
at both midpoint and endpoint level, in some impact and damages categories, the savings determined 
by the production of paper-wool were higher than the impacts resulted from the production of the 
insulation batts. These results were observed especially in the impact categories CC and FD and in 
the damage category Resources. Moreover, it is worth noting, that the study confirmed the high 
potential and efficiency of the application of assessment tools such as LCA from the very beginning 
of the product development process. Only by an early assessment it is possible to intervene effectively 
on all fronts. For instance, for what concern the product under study, revolutionary variations are still 
possible since no commitment is undertaken (e.g. no manufacturing process already in place). 
 
To conclude, it is important to give priority and focus on the insulation performances of the thermal 
insulation batts. In this study, the use phase was disregarded and the insulation performances were 
assumed to be the same for the four compositions and equal to the paper-wool. In fact, the assessment 
of the four insulation batts was based only on the economic and on the environmental performances, 
taking for granted their insulation capabilities. In accordance with Schmidt et al. (2004), during the 
use phase at least 100 times the environmental impacts related to the production and the disposal of 
the batts are saved with heating energy. Consequently, the main focus of the R&D department should 
be, without disregarding the environmental impacts when efficient alternatives are available, on 
developing the best insulating performing batt.

==PAGE:147==
 
Appendix B 
 
Assumptions to build the model 
 
Plasterboard waste Recycling & Refining unit  
 
The company provided the data about the Plasterboard waste recycling and refining unit. The data 
were about a general production day with an average plasterboard waste quality. For 100 kg of 
plasterboard waste the unit recovers 80 kg of gypsum, 1 kg of ferrous metals and 1 kg of dust from 
the filters. 20 kg of contaminated paper (with gypsum, plastic and wood) is the output and 100g of 
dust are emitted directly to air. For the modelling these data were scaled to the functional unit. Since 
these data were not measured directly during this master thesis it is assumed that they are valid and 
representative. And that no other outputs occur during the recovery of the gypsum in this unit. 
 
In the model and in the study, the plasterboard waste coming to the company for the recycling 
treatment is assumed to be burden free for all the calculations. 
 
PRU (paper recycling unit) and Manufacturing of paper-wool  
 
The company provided the data about the PRU (paper recycling unit) and Manufacturing of paper-
wool unit. The data covered an average production day and were measured twice. The following table 
summarizes the data. 
 
Production per hour 
First measurements [kg] 
Second measurements [kg] 
Clean paper 
1024 
991 
Heavy fraction 
1170 
1104 
Filter 1 
385 
340 
Filter 2 
55 
39 
Filter 3 
84 
69 
Light fraction 
78 
75 
Baffel 
124 
103 
 
The total inputs for the first and second measurements are 2920 kg and 2721 kg of contaminated 
paper, respectively. The output of Filter 1 is composed by the finer dust, the ones of Filter 2 and Filter 
3 are composed by dust and gypsum, the output of the baffel is composed by paint chips and dust 
mainly. The heavy fraction is composed by wood and non-ferrous metals. The light fraction is 
composed by paper non-adapt for the production of paper-wool and plastic chips. Also an emission 
of dust during the process was assumed equal to 100g per 1,07kg of paper-wool produced since 
unmeasurable. For the calculations, it was assumed that the heavy fractions and the light fraction are 
composed by 50% gypsum, 33,3% wood and 16,7% light plastic (mass). 
 
 
 
==PAGE:148==
APPENDIX B 
 
132
Emission of Dust to air (not filtered) 
 
During this process, it was not possible to quantify the amount of dust released to air during the 
working process of the first two units, i.e. Plasterboard waste Recycling & Refining unit and PRU 
(paper recycling unit) and Manufacturing of paper-wool unit. However, for modelling purpose it was 
decided to consider a certain amount of emissions to air in each unit process. In fact, during the 
production of paper-wool and the cleaning of the C&D waste some amounts of dust are released 
directly to air even if filters are present during the process. This unquantifiable amount of dust can 
also vary a lot with the weather and with the water content of the product processed. For these reasons 
it was decided to consider a small amount emitted to air and to further assess its influence on the 
results through a sensitivity analysis. The average amount of 100g of dust emitted to air to fulfil the 
functional unit was directly suggested by an expert of the company. 
 
Insulation Batts manufacturing  
 
During the insulation batts manufacturing process it is assumed that no emission to air, by-product 
and waste occur a part from the producing of the wanted insulation batt. Furthermore, for the pulp 
approach no reuse of part of the water for the pulp production is assumed. 
 
Calculations of the heat needed for the third and fourth insulation batt composition. 
 
First of all, it is assumed that the water used to make the pulp and prepare the insulation batts it is at 
20°C and a pressure of 1 atm. There are 2 kg of water in each batt to fulfil the defined functional unit. 
However, it is also assumed that 30% of the water leaves the insulation batts for dripping. 
Consequently, the total amount of water to evaporate is 1.4 kg. 
 
Energy needed to bring the water to the boiling point (from 20°C to 100°C) 
 
E1 = m * c * ΔT = 1.40 [kg] * 4.186 [kJ/kg] * (100 [°C] – 20 [°C]) = 468.832 [kJ] 
 
Energy needed to completely evaporate the water: 
 
E2 = 2272 [kJ/kg] * 1.40 [kg] = 3180.8 [kJ]  
 
Total Energy 
 
E1 + E2 = 3.65 [MJ] 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:149==
APPENDIX B 
 
133
Transport 
 
No data about the transport were available. Since the main scope of this project is to assess the various 
compositions it was decided to consider the transport only for the various materials implemented in 
the four compositions. It is also important to note that the 3 production process (the first three units) 
would be installed in series so no transport would be necessary.  
 
For what concerns the first insulation batts composition the two materials for which the transport is 
considered are the glue (Vinyl acetate) and the wood fibres used as binders. Both the materials are 
supposed to be delivered from Germany, the assumption is justified by the fact that the company 
delivering the wood fibres for the experiments was German and also that the provider of the glue it 
is likely be a German company. For this reason 750 kilometres of distance were assumed in the model 
for both products and delivered by a truck EURO 5 (lorry > 32 metric ton). 
 
For what concern the second insulation batt composition the two material for which the transport was 
modelled and considered are Cellulose ether and the kenaf fibres. The latter were delivered by an 
Italian company so 1750 kilometres of distance were assumed in the model and delivered by a truck 
EURO 5 (lorry > 32 metric ton). The cellulose ether binder was delivered by a Chinese company, it 
was assumed that this binder is delivered by a transoceanic ship, covering 20000 km (from port to 
port). 
 
Regarding the third insulation batt composition it was assumed that the Cement was delivered by a 
big Danish company producing Portland cement situated in Aalborg. The assumed distance was of 
400 km covered by truck EURO 5 (lorry > 32 metric ton).  
 
For what concern the fourth insulation batt composition as for the other combination studied, the 
gypsum fraction is supposed to be delivered by truck EURO 5 (lorry > 32 metric ton). For this material 
the kilometers assumed of transportation are 100.
